The Meaning of 'La Ilaha Illa Allah' and the Two Pillars of Uluhiyya
Sanusi defines the meaning of 'la ilaha illa Allah' and explains the two aspects of uluhiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the testimony of faith encapsulates all Islamic beliefs and that uluhiyya is defined by two attributes: absolute self-sufficiency (istighna) and absolute dependence (iftiqar).

Core Idea
The shahada defines God's independence and creation's dependence.

Sanusi now shows how the entire creed is summarised in the declaration of faith. He explains that the meaning of divinity (uluhiyya) has two essential aspects: God's complete independence from everything else, and everything else's complete dependence on Him. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows why it is foundational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice that Sanusi begins by stating that all beliefs are gathered in the shahada. Then he defines uluhiyya, then explains the meaning of the declaration.
2
Identify the two aspects of uluhiyya
Find the words 'istighna' and 'iftiqar' in the Arabic. Understand that they form the core definition: God is self-sufficient, all else is dependent.
These two concepts are the foundation for deriving all attributes.
3
Reflect on the logical implication
Ask yourself: If God is the only one who is independent and all else depends on Him, what does that say about worship? This connects to the first principle of tawhid.
The passage doesn't go further, but this sets the stage.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the quiz question.
The answer is B.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجْمَعُ مَعَانِي هذهِ العَقَائدِ كُلِّهَا قَوْلُ: لا إله إلا الله مُحمَّدٌ رَسُولُ الله؛ إذْ مَعْنَى الألوهِيَّةِ: استغناء الإلَهِ عِنْ كُلّ ما سواه، وافتقارُ كلّ مَا عَدَاهُ إلَيْهِ. فَمَعْنى لا إله إلا الله لا مُسْتَغْنياً عنْ مَا سِواه، ومُفْتَقِراً إليهِ كُلُّ مَا عَدَاهُ إِلا الله تعالى.
And the meanings of all these beliefs are gathered by the statement: 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'; for the meaning of divinity (al-uluhiyya) is the self-sufficiency of the god (istighna' al-ilah) from everything other than Him, and the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him. So the meaning of 'there is no god but Allah' is: there is no one who is self-sufficient from what is other than him and upon whom everything other than him depends except Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the core Islamic declaration and explains how it encapsulates all theological beliefs. Sanusi first states that all the beliefs (al-ʻaqāʼid) mentioned earlier are summarised in the shahada. He then defines the meaning of ‘ilāh’ in two aspects: istighnā’ (absolute self-sufficiency) and iftiqār (absolute dependence). The declaration ‘lā ilāha illā Allāh’ thus means: there is no being who is completely independent of all else and to whom all else is entirely dependent except Allah. This definition sets the foundation for deriving all necessary attributes of Allah from these two principles.

Key Terms
ألوهية
استغناء
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'ilāh' simply means 'worthy of worship' without understanding the deeper meanings of istighna and iftiqar.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly defines uluhiyya as the combination of absolute self-sufficiency and absolute dependence, not merely worship-worthiness.

Checkpoint
What are the two aspects that Sanusi states constitute the meaning of uluhiyya (divinity)?
A) Wujud and Qidam
B) Istighna and Iftiqar
C) Qudra and Irada
D) Tawhid and Risala

Key Takeaway
The shahada is the summary of all beliefs because it affirms that only Allah possesses absolute independence and absolute dependence, defining the essence of divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have recited 'La ilaha illa Allah' countless times. But have you ever truly felt the weight of the fact that you are completely dependent on Him for every breath, every ability, every moment? How does that realization change the way you approach your next decision?
?
If the meaning of divinity includes absolute self-sufficiency, then Allah needs nothing from you—not your worship, not your obedience. Yet you often act as if your worship is a favor to Him. What would it mean to live today as if your worship is purely your own need, not His?
?
Sanusi says the shahada gathers all beliefs. Is your daily life gathered around that single declaration? If you were to truly live as someone utterly dependent on Allah and aware of His complete independence, what one habit would you change today?

Lexical and Theological Nuances in the Opening: Kitab, Aziz, and Amalan
Bajuri defines 'kitab' and 'aziz' and explains the choice of 'amalan' over 'iqtida'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific lexical and theological meanings of the terms 'kitab', 'aziz', and the reason for the choice of 'amalan' over 'iqtida' in the author's wording.

Core Idea
Words are carefully chosen to convey precise theological meaning.

In this passage, the commentator Bajuri unpacks three key terms from the author's opening sentence. By examining the lexical history and theological implications of 'kitab', 'aziz', and the careful choice between 'amalan' and 'iqtida', we gain insight into the precision of Islamic theological discourse. Let's delve into Bajuri's explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Bajuri uses a very precise and compressed style.
2
Understand the definition of 'kitab'
Bajuri explains that 'kitab' originally meant 'writing', then became a term for the Quran. Note the shift from literal to conventional meaning.
This shows how terms develop in Islamic scholarship.
3
Grasp the two meanings of 'aziz'
Bajuri says 'aziz' can mean 'incomparable' or 'overwhelming', and both are intended. This illustrates the use of homonyms in theological discourse.
4
Appreciate the nuance between 'amalan' and 'iqtida'
Bajuri explains that 'amalan' is used for hadith because it contains a command, while 'iqtida' would be used for the Quran. This shows sensitivity to textual differences.
This connects to the broader methodology of usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بِالْكِتَابِ) مصدرٌ مزيد لـ: كَتَبَ أُطْلِقَ على المكتوب، وهو المنقوش، ثم أطلق على الألفاظ المخصوصة المنزلة على سيدنا محمد ، المتعبد بتلاوتها، المتحدى بأقصر سورة منها، ثم صار حقيقةً عرفيةً في ذلك، والمراد بمنزل الكتاب لما ذكرناه في غير موضع. (قوله: الْعَزِيزِ) أي: الذي لا نظير له، أو الغالِبُ على غيره، ويصح إرادة كلّ من المعنيين استعمالاً للمشترك في معنييه (قوله: وَعَمَلاً) إنما عَبر بالعمل هنا، وبالاقتداء ثُمَّ؛ لتضمن الخبر الأمر، بخلاف القرآن، فإنه لم يتضمنه كتضمن الخبر.
(His statement: 'bi-al-kitab'): It is an augmented verbal noun from 'kataba', applied to the written thing, i.e., what is inscribed. Then it was applied to the specific words revealed to our master Muhammad, which are worshipfully recited, challenged by the shortest sura thereof. Then it became a conventional reality in that sense. And what is meant by the Revealer of the Book is as we mentioned elsewhere. (His statement: 'al-aziz'): i.e., the one with no equal, or the one who overcomes others. And it is valid to intend both meanings, using the homonym in both its senses. (His statement: 'wa 'amalan'): He only expressed it with 'amal' (action) here and with 'iqtida' (emulation) previously because the hadith contains a command, unlike the Quran, because it does not contain a command in the same way the hadith does.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage provides lexical and theological commentary on three key words in the author's opening. First, 'kitab' (book): originally a verbal noun meaning 'writing', it was applied to written objects, then specifically to the revealed Quran, which is recited in worship and challenged by its shortest sura. It became a conventional term. Second, 'aziz' (mighty): it can mean either 'incomparable' or 'dominant/overwhelming', and both meanings are intended here. Third, the choice of 'amal' (action) instead of 'iqtida' in relation to the hadith is because the hadith explicitly contains a command (amr), whereas the Quranic text does not contain such a command in the same manner. Thus the wording reflects the difference between the two sources.

Key Terms
كتاب
عزيز
عمل
اقتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the term 'kitab' in the opening simply means any written book.
Correction: Bajuri clarifies that 'kitab' here is a conventional term for the Quran, not a literal written thing.

Checkpoint
Why does Bajuri say the author used 'amalan' in connection with the hadith instead of 'iqtida'an'?
A) Because 'amal' is more eloquent
B) Because the hadith contains a command
C) Because the Quran does not command anything
D) Because the hadith is weaker

Key Takeaway
The precise wording of theological texts carries deep lexical and methodological significance that must be carefully unpacked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you pause to consider the precise wording of the Quran and hadith, or do you let familiarity make you blind to the depth of each term? What would change if you treated every word as intentionally chosen?
?
The author chose 'amalan' over 'iqtida' because the hadith commands action. Look at your own practice: do you follow the sunnah as mere imitation, or do you feel the weight of the command behind it? Which mindset leads to deeper devotion?
?
If the word 'aziz' can mean both 'incomparable' and 'dominant' at once, how does that expand your understanding of Allah's might? Are you content with a one-dimensional view of His names, or will you strive to grasp their full richness?

Lexical Analysis of 'Kitab', 'Aziz', and 'Amal'
Lexical commentary on 'Kitab', 'Aziz', and 'Amal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical and terminological nuances of three key terms in the opening of Sanusi's al-Muqaddima: al-Kitab, al-Aziz, and wa 'amalan.

Core Idea
Words carry histories: 'Kitab', 'Aziz', and 'Amal' each have layered meanings that shape theological discourse.

Sanusi's matn begins with the phrase 'bi al-kitab al-aziz wa 'amalan' — 'by the Mighty Book and in accordance with'. Each word is teeming with lexical richness. The commentator unpacks these terms to show how the Qur'an and Sunnah are grounded in precise language, revealing the importance of word choice in theological formulation.

Guided Path
1
Trace the evolution of 'Kitab'
Read how the term 'kitab' started as a verbal noun and gradually became a conventional term for the revealed Qur'an. Notice the steps: from written object to the specific revealed words.
This shows how language can develop technical meanings.
2
Examine the two meanings of 'Aziz'
Identify the two interpretations: peerless or overwhelming. Understand that the word is a homonym and both meanings can be intended simultaneously.
This is an example of using a word in multiple senses.
3
Distinguish 'Amal' from 'Iqtida'
Understand why the commentator uses 'amal (action) for hadith and iqtida (following) for Qur'an. The reason lies in the nature of commands: hadith contains a command, while the Qur'an does not in that context.
This highlights the precision in legal terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بِالْكِتَابِ) مصدرٌ مزيد لـ: كَتَبَ أُطْلِقَ على المكتوب، وهو المنقوش، ثم أطلق على الألفاظ المخصوصة المنزلة على سيدنا محمد ، المتعبد بتلاوتها، المتحدى بأقصر سورة منها، ثم صار حقيقةً عرفيةً في ذلك، والمراد بمنزل الكتاب لما ذكرناه في غير موضع. (قوله: الْعَزِيزِ) أي: الذي لا نظير له، أو الغالِبُ على غيره، ويصح إرادة كلّ من المعنيين استعمالاً للمشترك في معنييه (قوله: وَعَمَلاً) إنما عَبر بالعمل هنا، وبالاقتداء ثُمَّ؛ لتضمن الخبر الأمر، بخلاف القرآن، فإنه لم يتضمنه كتضمن الخبر.
(His saying: 'by the Book') is an augmented verbal noun from kataba, originally applied to the written thing, which is inscribed, then applied to the specific words revealed to our master Muhammad, which are to be recited as worship, and which are challenged by the shortest sura thereof; then it became a conventional reality in that sense. And what is meant by 'the revealed Book' according to what we mentioned elsewhere. (His saying: 'the Mighty') meaning: that which has no peer, or the one that overpowers others; and it is valid to intend both meanings using the homonym in both its meanings. (His saying: 'and in accordance with') [i.e., acting upon] He only used 'amal (action) here and iqtida' (following) there because the report (hadith) implies a command, unlike the Qur'an, for it does not imply it as the report does.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a lexical commentary on three terms in the matn's opening. First, 'al-kitab' (the Book): the author explains that it is originally a verbal noun from kataba, then applied to the written object, then specifically to the revealed words of the Qur'an, becoming a conventional term for it. The matn uses it to refer to the Qur'an. Second, 'al-aziz' (the Mighty): two interpretations are given — either 'having no peer' or 'overpowering'; both are valid since the word is a homonym. Third, 'wa 'amalan' (and in accordance with): the author contrasts it with 'iqtida'an' (following) used for the Qur'an, explaining that the phrase 'in accordance with' is used for the hadith because the hadith contains a command (amr), whereas the Qur'an does not contain an implied command in that context.

Key Terms
الكتاب
العزيز
عرفية
حقيقة
مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'aziz' in 'bi al-kitab al-aziz' means 'dear' or 'beloved'.
Correction: It means 'mighty' in the sense of invincibility or uniqueness, not 'dear'.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the word 'al-aziz' in 'bi-al-kitab al-aziz' primarily mean?
A) The beloved book.
B) The book that cannot be imitated or the overwhelming book.
C) The book written by 'Aziz.
D) The book with a strong binding.

Key Takeaway
The opening words of Sanusi's matn are carefully chosen; 'Kitab' is a conventional term for the Qur'an, 'Aziz' can mean peerless or overwhelming, and 'Amal' signals that hadiths carry commands requiring action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear 'the Book' (al-Kitab), do you treat it as just any book or as the unique, peerless revelation that demands your action? This lesson shows that 'Kitab' is a conventional term specifically for the Qur'an. How does this awareness change the way you approach reading it today? Commit to reading the Qur'an today with the consciousness that it is 'Aziz' - having no peer - and let that awe shape your recitation.
?
The passage distinguishes between 'amal' (action) for hadith and 'iqtida' (following) for Quran. Are you merely following the Qur'an intellectually, or are you acting on what you learn from both Qur'an and hadith? Examine one hadith you know that contains a command - have you truly acted upon it? If not, what specific step will you take today to act?

Definition of Dhū Bāl and the Condition 'Shar‘an'
Definition of 'dhū bāl' and correction of condition to 'shar‘an'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'dhū bāl' as a matter of religious concern in kalām and why the commentator suggests the condition should be 'shar‘an' instead of 'ʿurfan'.

Core Idea
A matter of concern in kalām must be religiously significant, not merely customary.

In defining the subject matter of kalām, the commentator clarifies what qualifies as 'something of concern'. He insists that the attention must be according to the Sacred Law, excluding forbidden, disliked, or trivial matters. This precision ensures that kalām deals with weighty theological issues.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm, then read the translation carefully. Note that the passage has two parts: the definition and the commentator's remark on wording.
2
Identify the key conditions
Find the conditions that a matter must meet to be considered 'dhū bāl': it must be paid attention to according to the Sacred Law, not forbidden or disliked in itself, and not among futile things.
The commentator focuses on the phrase 'shar‘an'.
3
Understand the critique from the commentator
Read the part where the commentator says it would have been better to say 'ʿurfan'. Ask yourself: why would that be better? How does it relate to excluding futile matters?
4
Apply the definition
Think of examples of topics that would be considered 'dhū bāl' in kalām (e.g., attributes of Allah, prophethood) and those that would not (e.g., trivial daily affairs).
This helps internalize the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب حالٍ يُهْتَمُّ به شرعاً بحيث لا يكون مُحَرّماً ولا مكروهاً لذاتهما، ولا من سَفَاسِفِ الأمور - أي الأمور الخسيسة .
(قوله: يُهْتَمُّ بِهِ شَرْعاً) هذا بيان للحال، والضمير في يهتم به راجع للأمر، وكان الأولى أن يقول: وعرفاً؛ ليظهر إخراج ما هو من سفاسف الأمور بقوله: (ذي بال).
A matter of concern that is paid attention to according to the Sacred Law, such that it is not forbidden nor disliked in itself, nor among futile things—that is, base things. (His saying: 'is paid attention to according to the Sacred Law') This is an explanation of the condition, and the pronoun in 'is paid attention to' refers to the matter. It would have been better for him to say: 'and customarily', in order to show the exclusion of what is among futile things by his saying: 'of concern'.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'dhū bāl' (something of concern) in the context of the definition of kalām. It begins by stating that 'dhū bāl' is a matter that is paid attention to according to the Sacred Law (shar‘an), meaning it is not inherently forbidden or disliked, nor is it among base or futile matters. The commentator then explains that in the original text, the phrase 'paid attention to according to the Sacred Law' is meant to specify that the concern is religious, not merely customary. He notes that it would have been more appropriate to explicitly say 'and customarily' to make clear that trivial matters are excluded by the phrase 'of concern'. This precision is important because it distinguishes proper subjects of kalām from insignificant worldly affairs.

Key Terms
ذو بال
سفاسف الأمور
شرعاً
عرفاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'dhū bāl' includes any matter of interest or concern, regardless of religious significance.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'dhū bāl' must be according to the Sacred Law (shar‘an), meaning it must be of religious concern and not merely customary or worldly. The commentator suggests using 'ʿurfan' to emphasize the exclusion of futile matters.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say it would have been better to say 'urfan' instead of 'shar'an'?
A) Because 'urfan' is a broader term that includes all customs.
B) Because 'urfan' would exclude futile matters more explicitly.
C) Because the original text should be corrected for grammar.
D) Because 'shar'an' is too narrow and excludes valid customary concerns.

Key Takeaway
The term 'dhū bāl' in kalām refers to a matter of religious concern, not just any matter of interest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself worrying or arguing about a matter, do you stop to ask: 'Is this truly of religious concern, or is it merely a customary or trivial issue?'
?
Think of a recent debate you engaged in. Was the topic from the category of 'safāsif al-umūr'? How can you ensure your discussions are spiritually beneficial?
?
The commentator preferred 'customarily' over 'religiously' to exclude trivial matters. In your own life, do you sometimes dress up trivial religious matters as important? What would change if you honestly assessed whether they are truly 'dhū bāl'?

The Name and the Named: A Theological Distinction
Sanusi defines 'ism' and derives its meaning, showing it is other than the named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological definition of 'ism' (name) and its relationship to the named (musamma), including the two interpretive possibilities in the basmalah and their implications for divine attributes.

Core Idea
The name is not the same as the named—except when it is.

This passage tackles a seemingly simple question: what does the word 'name' mean? But it quickly leads to a deep theological distinction, one that has consequences for how we understand the basmalah and the divine attributes. Sanusi defines 'ism' not as a grammatical part of speech but as an indicator of a named entity, and then explores how this applies to the most holy essence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to capture the full flow. Notice the interplay between definition, derivation, and application.
The passage includes commentary from al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi, which expands on the core point.
2
Focus on the definition and derivation
Identify the theological definition of 'ism' (indicator of a named thing) and its two possible derivations (from elevation or from a sign). Consider why these derivations matter for understanding the relationship with the named.
The grammatical definition (opposed to verb/particle) is explicitly set aside.
3
Understand the two interpretations in the basmalah
Work through the two ways the word 'name' can be understood in the basmalah: either as referring to the divine names (other than the essence) or as referring to the essence itself (identical). Relate this to the core idea that the name can be other than or identical to the named depending on what is signified.
The first interpretation is considered the verification (tahqīq) by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الاسم: وهو ما دَلَّ على مسمّى، لا ما قابَلَ الفعل والحرف؛ لأن ذلك اصطلاح نحوي. وهو مشتق من السُّمُو بمعنى: العُلُوّ؛ لأنه يَعْلُو مسمّاه، أو من السَّمَةِ بمعنى: العلامة؛ لأنه علامة عليه. وعُلِمَ من التعريف المذكور: حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: يَعْلُو مُسَمَّاهُ) (مسماه) فاعل (يعلو)، ولو قال: يعلو به مسماه؛ لكان أوضح. (قوله: وَعُلِمَ مِنَ التَّعْرِيفِ... إلخ) حاصل ذلك : أن لفظ (اسم) الواقع في البسملة مثلاً؛ إما أن يراد به الأسماء الدالة على الذات الأقدس بأن يستعمل فيها، وتكون هي مدلولة له، فلفظ (اسم) غير المسمى، وهو الذات باعتبار مدلوله، وهو الأسماء المذكورة، وإما أن يراد بلفظ (اسم) الذات الأقدس، فهو حينئذ عين المسمى باعتبار مدلوله، وهو الذات، فمدلوله على الوجه الأول غير الذات، وذلك الغير هو الأسماء الدالة على تلك الذات، وعلى الثاني نفس الذات، وبهذا علم أن المحكوم عليه بأنه غير المسمى على القول الأول، وأنه عين المسمى على المقابل له مدلول لفظ الاسم. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي . فقد علمت من هذا: أن فيها أيضاً إشارةً إلى أدلة العقائد. قال بعضهم: لم يَتَبَيَّن من ذلك الإشارة إلى العقائد المتعلقة بالرُّسُل والسمعيات وبيانه أن تقول: إنكم قلتم : ابتدأ بها امتثالاً للأحاديث، والامتثالُ فَرْعُ تصديق المحدّث، وإذا صَدَقَ؛ فمن جملة أخباره أنهم معصومون، مبلغون، جائز في حقهم كلُّ ما لم يُنقض، وإذا ثبت ذلك؛ استحال ضده، وكذلك السمعيات، فإنها متعلقات من قبل المحدّث اهـ. وقد يقال: من جملة إنعامه: إرسالُ الرُّسُل المؤيَّدين بالقرآن والمعجزة التي هي من جُمْلَته أيضاً؛ إذ المدار على هذا: على مُطلَق الإشارة، لا على الاستلزام العقلي الذي سلكه المحقِّي وإن كان هو الأوجه.
The second: the name (al-ism): it is that which indicates a named thing (musamma), not what opposes the verb and particle; because that is a grammatical convention. It is derived from al-sumuww meaning elevation, because it elevates its named thing, or from al-sima meaning sign, because it is a sign upon it. It is known from the mentioned definition: the gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri (his saying: 'it elevates its named thing') — 'its named thing' is the subject of 'elevates', and if he had said 'elevates by it its named thing' it would be clearer. (His saying: 'And it is known from the definition... etc.') The summary of that: the word 'name' occurring in the basmalah, for example, either it is intended to mean the names indicative of the most holy essence by being used for them, and they are its signification, so the word 'name' is other than the named, which is the essence in terms of its signification, namely the mentioned names; or it is intended by the word 'name' the most holy essence itself, so then it is identical to the named in terms of its signification, which is the essence. So its signification on the first view is other than the essence, and that other is the names indicative of that essence, and on the second view it is the essence itself. And by this it is known that that which is judged to be other than the named on the first view, and identical to the named on the opposing view, is the signification of the word 'name'. The annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: You have known from this that there is also in it an indication to the proofs of the beliefs (adillat al-ʿaqāʾid). Some said: It is not apparent from that an indication to the beliefs pertaining to the messengers (al-rusul) and the revealed matters (al-samʿiyyāt). Its clarification: you say: Indeed you said: He began with it in compliance with the hadiths, and compliance is a branch of affirming the truth of the narrator (al-muḥaddith), and if he is truthful, then among his reports is that they are infallible, conveyers, and that is possible in their regard everything that does not contravene, and when that is established, its opposite becomes impossible; and likewise the revealed matters, for they are things dependent on the narrator. End. And it may be said: Among His blessings is the sending of messengers supported by the Quran and the miracle that is also part of it; for the criterion here is absolute indication, not the rational entailment that the verifier followed, though it is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ism' (name) and clarifies its relationship with the named (musamma), a key theological issue in the context of divine names. The definition is presented as the second of two undefined terms in the basmalah: 'ism' is that which indicates a named entity, distinguishing it from grammatical definitions of noun. Its derivation is either from 'sumuww' (elevation) because it elevates its named entity, or from 'sima' (sign) because it serves as a sign. The commentary by al-Ajhuri then explains the implications: in the basmalah, the word 'name' can either refer to the names indicating the divine essence (in which case it is other than the named essence) or to the essence itself (in which case it is identical). The first interpretation is deemed the verification (tahqīq) because it accords with the original usage of the term. Al-Anbabi's annotations then draw an indirect connection to theological proofs: the basmalah's use of 'name' can, through the chain of compliance and affirmation of the Prophet, indicate the proofs of prophethood and revealed matters, though this indication is not direct rational entailment but a broader reference.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
الذات
التعريف
السمو
السّمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'ism' (name) always means the same as the named entity.
Correction: In theological context, 'ism' can be other than the named (when referring to the divine names) or identical to the named (when referring to the essence itself).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two possible roots from which 'ism' is derived?
A) Sumuww (elevation) and sima (sign)
B) Sumuww (elevation) and ism (noun)
C) Sima (sign) and fi‘l (verb)
D) Harf (particle) and ism (name)

Key Takeaway
The word 'ism' can be understood as either the divine names (other than the essence) or the essence itself (identical), a distinction that shapes theological discussions of the basmalah and divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Bismillah' before an action, which of the two interpretations do you subconsciously adopt? Does it change the way you begin if you see the name as other than the essence, pointing to the divine names, or as identical to the essence itself? What does your answer reveal about your actual consciousness of Allah?
?
The name is said to 'elevate its named thing'. How does pronouncing the names of Allah—like 'Ar-Rahman' or 'Al-‘Aleem'—affect your perception of His greatness? Does your tongue speak them while your heart remains low? What one change will you make today to let the names elevate your soul?
?
This lesson draws a chain from the basmalah to affirming prophethood. When you say 'Bismillah', do you implicitly affirm your trust in the Prophet's truthfulness? If the basmalah is your first step in an action, is your next step obedience to the one who taught it? Where in your life is there a gap between the basmalah you speak and the submission it demands?

Definition of Al-Hamd: Linguistic and Terminological
Sanusi defines al-hamd linguistically and terminologically, addressing an objection about inner belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of al-hamd in Arabic linguistics and Islamic theology, and why it encompasses speech, belief, and action.

Core Idea
Al-hamd is not just words—it is any act that shows glorification of Allah as the Benefactor.

Sanusi begins his famous matn by defining the very act of praising Allah. He provides both a linguistic and a technical definition, then addresses a subtle objection about whether inner belief can be considered a form of praise. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding why genuine praise must come from the heart, tongue, and limbs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get a feel for the author's flow.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the linguistic definition (لغةً) and the terminological definition (اصطلاحاً). Notice the key terms: الثناء, الجميل, الاختياري, التعظيم, الإنباء, المنعم.
The linguistic definition focuses on praise for a voluntary beautiful act; the terminological definition broadens it to any act indicating glorification of the Benefactor.
3
Understand the three modes
The terminological definition says hamd can be by speech, belief, or action. Reflect on why belief alone qualifies and how it relates to external expression.
The verse illustrates the three modes: hand (action), tongue (speech), conscience (belief).
4
Analyze the objection and answer
Read the objection (فإن قيل) and the answer (أجيب). The objection says we cannot know someone's inner belief. The answer explains that external indications (قرائن الأحوال) reveal it.
This shows that hamd must be knowable to others through signs, even if the core belief is hidden.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this definition matter for worship? How does it connect to the idea that actions and words must be accompanied by inner faith?
This prepares you for later discussions on sincerity and the conditions of acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحمد لغةً: هو الثناء بالجميل على الجميل الاختياري على جهة التعظيم. واصطلاحاً: فِعْلٌ يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنْعِم بسبب كونه مُنْعِماً على الحامد أو غيره، سواء كان ذلك الفعل قولاً باللسان، أو اعتقاداً بالجنان، أو عملاً بالأركان، كما قيل: أَفَادَتْكُمُ النَّعْمَاءُ مِنِّي ثَلَاثَةٌ يَدِي وَلِسَانِي وَالضَّمِيرَ الْمُحَجَّبَا فإن قيل: لا اطلاع لنا على الاعتقاد حتى يُنبئ عن تعظيم المنعم. أجيب: بأنه وإن كان لا اطلاع لنا عليه؛ لكن تَدلُّنا عليه قرائن الأحوال.
And 'al-hamd' linguistically: it is praise of the beautiful for a voluntary beautiful [act] in a manner of glorification. And terminologically: an act that informs of glorifying the Benefactor because of His being a benefactor to the one praising or others, whether that act is a word by the tongue, or a belief by the heart, or an action by the limbs, as it was said: 'The favors from me have benefited you three: my hand, my tongue, and the concealed conscience.' Then if it is said: We have no knowledge of the belief so that it would inform of glorifying the Benefactor. The answer: that even though we have no knowledge of it, yet indications of the circumstances indicate it to us.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage from Sanusi's matn provides a two-part definition of 'al-hamd' (praise). First, the linguistic definition: praise (الثناء) of the beautiful (الجميل) for a voluntary beautiful act (الجميل الاختياري) in a manner of glorification (على جهة التعظيم). The term 'الجميل' refers to the praised attribute or act. 'الاختياري' qualifies the act, meaning it must be voluntary; involuntary beauty like a nice appearance is not praised. 'على جهة التعظيم' means the praise must be in the manner of glorification, not merely flattery. Second, the terminological definition: hamd is an act that indicates (ينبئ) glorification (تعظيم) of the Benefactor (المنعم) because He is a benefactor. This act can be by speech, belief, or action. The poet's verse illustrates these three: hand (action), tongue (speech), and conscience (belief). Then an objection is raised: we cannot know the inner belief of a person, so how can the act 'inform' of glorification if belief is internal? The answer is that while we do not have direct knowledge, external indications (قرائن الأحوال) provide evidence. Thus, the definition holds.

Key Terms
الحمد
الثناء
الجميل
الاختياري
التعظيم
المنعم
الإنباء
الاعتقاد
القرائن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that hamd (praise) is only expressed verbally, through speech.
Correction: Sanusi's definition explicitly includes belief by the heart and action by the limbs, not just speech.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a way hamd can be expressed according to Sanusi's terminological definition?
A) قول باللسان (speech by tongue)
B) اعتقاد بالجنان (belief by heart)
C) عمل بالأركان (action by limbs)
D) مجرد النية دون عمل (mere intention without action)

Key Takeaway
Al-hamd is any act—speech, belief, or action—that informs of glorifying Allah as the Benefactor, and even inner belief is evidenced by external indications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your praise of Allah often flows from your tongue during prayer, but the definition of hamd includes belief and action. Which of these three—speech, heart, or limbs—do you find yourself neglecting most in your daily glorification of Allah? What one concrete act will you add today to make your hamd complete?
?
The objection in this lesson questions how inner belief can be known, but Allah knows what is in your heart. When you praise Allah with your lips but your heart is distant, does that praise still count as hamd? What if you treated your inner conviction as the true measure of your praise—how would your worship change?
?
The source says that even hidden beliefs are indicated by circumstances. If someone watched your actions and habits for a week, would they conclude that you truly glorify Allah as your Benefactor? What specific outward sign of your inner belief is missing, and what can you do to align your actions with the hamd on your tongue?

The Qualifier al-Ikhtiyari: Hamd vs Madh
Al-Ajhuri explains the qualifier 'al-ikhtiyari' and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the qualifier 'al-ikhtiyari' is essential in the definition of hamd and how it distinguishes hamd from madh.

Core Idea
Hamd praises voluntary acts; madh praises inherent qualities.

In defining hamd, Sanusi includes the qualifier 'al-ikhtiyari' (voluntary) to specify that the praised act must be voluntary. Al-Ajhuri explains that this qualifier applies only to the act being praised, not to the person praised. He gives an example to distinguish hamd from madh: praising someone for a voluntary act is hamd, while praising for a non-voluntary quality is madh. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage on the qualifier and its translation. Notice how al-Ajhuri explains that 'al-ikhtiyari' is a condition only for the praise-worthy act.
2
Examine the example
Study the example: saying 'Zayd is good because he honored you' (hamd) vs 'Zayd is generous because he is handsome' (madh). Identify why the first is hamd and the second madh.
The key difference is whether the cause is voluntary or not.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider: Why does this matter for theology? Hamd is reserved for Allah's voluntary acts, not for His essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: الاختياري) هو قيد للمحمود عليه فقط، ولهذا لم يذكره بعد المحمود به، فإذا قلت: زيد حسن لكونه أكرمك فهو حمد بخلاف ما إذا قلت: زيد كريم لكونه حسناً فهو مدح.
(وقوله: عَلَى جِهَةِ التَّعْظِيم) المراد به أن لا يأتي بمناف للتعظيم وإن لم يعتقد عظمة المحمود.
(قوله: بِسَبب) متعلق بـ (فعل) أو بمحذوف، والتقدير: صادر ذلك الفعل بسبب... إلخ.
(وقوله: عَلَى الْحَامِدِ) ليس هذا من تتمة التعريف لئلا يلزم الدور، وكذا التعميم بعده.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي [؟].
الاجتماعية القائمة بالمجموع؛ ففيه أن المقصود: الحكم على الأفراد، لا على الهيئة، وكذا يُقال فيما بعد.
(قوله: أَوِ اعْتِقَاداً (بِالْجَنَانِ المراد بالاعتقاد: اعتقادُ نَحْوِ العلم والكرم، لا اعتقاد العَظَمَة، وإلا؛ لَزِمَ إنباء الشيء عن نفسه؛ لأن المراد بالتعظيم في قولهم: يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنْعِم : اعتقادُ العَظَمَة، وجَعْلُ الاعتقاد فِعْلاً إنما هو بحسب العُزف [؟]، وجَعْلُه من قبيل الكيف لا الفعل تدقيق فلسفي.
Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: al-ikhtiyari) is a restriction for the praised act only, and for this he did not mention it after the praised one. So if you say: Zayd is good because he honored you, that is hamd. Unlike if you say: Zayd is generous because he is handsome, that is madh. (And his saying: on the manner of glorification) what is meant by it is that he does not bring anything contrary to glorification even if he does not believe in the greatness of the praised. (His saying: because) is connected to 'act' or to an omitted word, and the estimation: that act occurred because... etc. (His saying: to the one praising) is not part of the definition to avoid circularity, and likewise the generalization after it. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: The social whole standing by the aggregate; in it is that the intended is the judgment on the individuals, not on the form, and likewise is said later. (His saying: or a belief by the heart) what is meant by belief is belief in something like knowledge and generosity, not belief in greatness; otherwise it would entail the thing informing about itself, because what is meant by glorification in their saying 'informs of glorifying the Benefactor' is belief in greatness, and making belief an act is only according to custom, and making it of the category of quality not act is philosophical precision.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya provides commentary on various parts of Sanusi's definition of hamd. Al-Ajhuri first explains the qualifier 'الاختياري' (voluntary). It applies only to the act being praised (المحمود عليه), not to the person praised. For example, praising Zayd for voluntarily honoring you is hamd, but praising him for being handsome (which is not voluntary) is mere madh (praise not necessarily involving glorification). Next, 'عَلَى جِهَةِ التَّعْظِيم' (on the manner of glorification) means that the act of praising must not involve anything contradictory to glorification, even if the one praising does not believe in the greatness of the praised. It is about the form of praise, not the inner conviction. Then, the word 'بسبب' (because) is syntactically connected to 'فعل' or a deleted word, meaning the act is because of the beneficence. The phrase 'عَلَى الْحَامِدِ' (to the one praising) is deliberately not part of the definition to avoid circularity (al-dawr), because if 'benefactor' is defined in relation to the one praising, it would be circular. The generalization (التعميم) after it (i.e., 'or other') is also not part of the essential definition. Al-Anbabi then comments on the phrase 'social whole standing by the aggregate' – this seems to refer to the earlier discussion about the composite of eternal and temporal praise; the judgment is on individuals, not on the collective form. Finally, on the phrase 'or a belief by the heart', he clarifies that 'belief' here means belief in the benefactor's knowledge and generosity, not belief in His greatness (العظمة). If it were belief in greatness, then the definition would be circular because 'informing of glorification' already implies belief in greatness. Additionally, regarding whether belief is an 'act', he notes that technically belief is a quality (كيف), not an act, but it is called an act in a customary sense (بحسب العرف), not in the precise philosophical sense.

Key Terms
الاختياري
المحمود عليه
المحمود به
مدح
حمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think hamd and madh are the same, but this passage shows they differ in the object of praise: hamd requires a voluntary act, madh does not.
Correction: Hamd is specifically for voluntary acts; madh can be for any praiseworthy quality, voluntary or not.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri, which of the following is an example of hamd?
A) Saying 'Zayd is generous because he is handsome'
B) Saying 'Zayd is good because he honored you'
C) Saying 'Zayd is tall'
D) Saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable'

Key Takeaway
Hamd is praise for a voluntary act of beneficence, unlike madh which can be for any non-voluntary quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you praised someone recently. Was your praise for a voluntary act they chose to do, or for a quality they were born with? How does that distinction affect the sincerity of your praise? This week, when you say alhamdulillah, pause and ask: is this praise specifically for a voluntary act of Allah's beneficence?
?
The lesson shows that hamd is higher than madh because it acknowledges a voluntary choice. Consider your own desire for praise: do you prefer to be praised for what you have chosen to do, or for qualities you did not earn? Which type of praise is more lasting and why? Make a commitment today to seek only such praise that is due for voluntary good deeds.

Glorification and Avoiding Circularity
Al-Ajhuri explains three points: glorification condition, syntactic connection, and exclusion to avoid circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'ala jihah al-ta'dhim' (in a manner of glorification) and why the phrase 'ala al-hamid' is excluded to prevent circularity.

Core Idea
Glorification in hamd is about the form of praise, not inner belief; the definition avoids circularity by not making the benefactor depend on the one praising.

After discussing the voluntary qualifier, al-Ajhuri clarifies two other parts of the definition: 'ala jihah al-ta'dhim' and the syntactic connection of 'bisa sabab' and the exclusion of 'ala al-hamid'. He explains that the praise must be uttered in a manner that does not contradict glorification, even if the speaker does not believe in the greatness of the praised. Then he notes that the phrase 'ala al-hamid' was intentionally omitted to prevent circular reasoning. This lesson examines these linguistic and logical points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition of glorification
Read the part on 'ala jihah al-ta'dhim'. Note that the condition is about the outward form of praise, not the inner conviction.
Even if the speaker does not believe in the greatness, as long as the words don't contradict glorification, it counts as hamd.
2
Syntactic connection of 'bisa sabab'
Examine how 'bisa sabab' is connected to 'act' or an omitted word, indicating the reason for the praise.
3
Examine the exclusion of 'ala al-hamid'
Understand why 'ala al-hamid' is not part of the definition: it would create a circular definition (al-dawr). Also, the generalization after it is excluded.
Circularity would occur if 'benefactor' is defined relative to the one praising.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: الاختياري) هو قيد للمحمود عليه فقط، ولهذا لم يذكره بعد المحمود به، فإذا قلت: زيد حسن لكونه أكرمك فهو حمد بخلاف ما إذا قلت: زيد كريم لكونه حسناً فهو مدح.
(وقوله: عَلَى جِهَةِ التَّعْظِيم) المراد به أن لا يأتي بمناف للتعظيم وإن لم يعتقد عظمة المحمود.
(قوله: بِسَبب) متعلق بـ (فعل) أو بمحذوف، والتقدير: صادر ذلك الفعل بسبب... إلخ.
(وقوله: عَلَى الْحَامِدِ) ليس هذا من تتمة التعريف لئلا يلزم الدور، وكذا التعميم بعده.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي [؟].
الاجتماعية القائمة بالمجموع؛ ففيه أن المقصود: الحكم على الأفراد، لا على الهيئة، وكذا يُقال فيما بعد.
(قوله: أَوِ اعْتِقَاداً (بِالْجَنَانِ المراد بالاعتقاد: اعتقادُ نَحْوِ العلم والكرم، لا اعتقاد العَظَمَة، وإلا؛ لَزِمَ إنباء الشيء عن نفسه؛ لأن المراد بالتعظيم في قولهم: يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنْعِم : اعتقادُ العَظَمَة، وجَعْلُ الاعتقاد فِعْلاً إنما هو بحسب العُزف [؟]، وجَعْلُه من قبيل الكيف لا الفعل تدقيق فلسفي.
Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: al-ikhtiyari) is a restriction for the praised act only, and for this he did not mention it after the praised one. So if you say: Zayd is good because he honored you, that is hamd. Unlike if you say: Zayd is generous because he is handsome, that is madh. (And his saying: on the manner of glorification) what is meant by it is that he does not bring anything contrary to glorification even if he does not believe in the greatness of the praised. (His saying: because) is connected to 'act' or to an omitted word, and the estimation: that act occurred because... etc. (His saying: to the one praising) is not part of the definition to avoid circularity, and likewise the generalization after it. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: The social whole standing by the aggregate; in it is that the intended is the judgment on the individuals, not on the form, and likewise is said later. (His saying: or a belief by the heart) what is meant by belief is belief in something like knowledge and generosity, not belief in greatness; otherwise it would entail the thing informing about itself, because what is meant by glorification in their saying 'informs of glorifying the Benefactor' is belief in greatness, and making belief an act is only according to custom, and making it of the category of quality not act is philosophical precision.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya provides commentary on various parts of Sanusi's definition of hamd. Al-Ajhuri first explains the qualifier 'الاختياري' (voluntary). It applies only to the act being praised (المحمود عليه), not to the person praised. For example, praising Zayd for voluntarily honoring you is hamd, but praising him for being handsome (which is not voluntary) is mere madh (praise not necessarily involving glorification). Next, 'عَلَى جِهَةِ التَّعْظِيم' (on the manner of glorification) means that the act of praising must not involve anything contradictory to glorification, even if the one praising does not believe in the greatness of the praised. It is about the form of praise, not the inner conviction. Then, the word 'بسبب' (because) is syntactically connected to 'فعل' or a deleted word, meaning the act is because of the beneficence. The phrase 'عَلَى الْحَامِدِ' (to the one praising) is deliberately not part of the definition to avoid circularity (al-dawr), because if 'benefactor' is defined in relation to the one praising, it would be circular. The generalization (التعميم) after it (i.e., 'or other') is also not part of the essential definition. Al-Anbabi then comments on the phrase 'social whole standing by the aggregate' – this seems to refer to the earlier discussion about the composite of eternal and temporal praise; the judgment is on individuals, not on the collective form. Finally, on the phrase 'or a belief by the heart', he clarifies that 'belief' here means belief in the benefactor's knowledge and generosity, not belief in His greatness (العظمة). If it were belief in greatness, then the definition would be circular because 'informing of glorification' already implies belief in greatness. Additionally, regarding whether belief is an 'act', he notes that technically belief is a quality (كيف), not an act, but it is called an act in a customary sense (بحسب العرف), not in the precise philosophical sense.

Key Terms
تعظيم
بسبب
دور
تعميم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hamd requires inner belief in the greatness of the praised.
Correction: The commentary states that even without inner belief, as long as the words do not contradict glorification, it is still hamd.

Checkpoint
Why is the phrase 'ala al-hamid' (to the one praising) not part of the definition?
A) It is grammatically incorrect
B) It would create circularity
C) It is redundant
D) It contradicts glorification

Key Takeaway
Hamd requires only an outward expression of glorification, and the definition avoids circularity by not including the one praising.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah in your daily adhkar or salah, do you consider the outward expression more than your inner conviction? Are there times when your lips utter hamd but your heart feels distant from the glory you speak? How can you ensure your words of praise are truly on the manner of glorification, not contradicting the majesty they describe?
?
The definition of hamd excludes the one praising to avoid circularity. In your worship, is your praise of Allah sometimes circular—conditional upon what you have received or hope to receive? How can you train yourself to praise Him purely because of who He is, not because of the benefaction you experience?
?
If the phrase 'to the one praising' were included in the definition, it would make the definition invalid. Similarly, when you reflect on your relationship with Allah, does your devotion revolve around your own benefit? How can you break free from a self-centered approach to worship and focus entirely on Allah's greatness?

Al-Anbabi's Notes: Social Whole and Belief
Al-Anbabi comments on the social whole and clarifies the meaning of belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Anbabi's clarification that the judgment in hamd is on individuals, and the nature of belief in the definition of hamd.

Core Idea
The definition of hamd applies to each individual act of praise, not to a collective form, and 'belief' means belief in beneficence, not in greatness.

Al-Anbabi adds two important clarifications. First, regarding the earlier mention of the 'social whole', he notes that the intended judgment is on the individuals, not on the collective form. Second, he explains that the term 'belief' in the definition refers to belief in the benefactor's knowledge and generosity, not belief in His greatness; otherwise the definition would be circular. He also notes that while belief is technically a quality (kayf), it is called an act in a customary sense. This lesson unpacks these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the social whole point
Read the part about 'al-ijtima'iyyah al-qa'imah bi al-majmu''. Al-Anbabi clarifies that the judgment is on the individuals, not on the collective form (al-hay'ah).
This likely refers to the earlier composite of eternal and temporal praise; the ruling applies to each praise individually.
2
Examine the clarification about belief
Study the statement about 'i'tiqad'. Note that belief here means belief in the benefactor's knowledge and generosity, not belief in greatness.
If it were belief in greatness, it would cause circularity because 'informs of glorifying' already implies greatness.
3
Reflect on the act vs quality distinction
Notice that al-Anbabi mentions that calling belief an 'act' is customary, but philosophically it is a quality (kayf).
This is a precise philosophical refinement.

?

Definition of Hamd: Linguistic and Terminological
Sanusi defines hamd linguistically and terminologically, then answers an objection about inner belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual definition of hamd (praise) and how it encompasses speech, belief, and action, as well as the answer to the objection regarding our knowledge of inner belief.

Core Idea
Praise is an act honoring the Benefactor, whether spoken, believed, or done.

Sanusi begins by establishing what praise (ḥamd) means in both language and theological usage. He distinguishes voluntary acts of beauty and then breaks praise into three categories: speech, inner conviction, and physical action. The passage also anticipates and resolves a potential objection about our inability to directly perceive another's inner belief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic definition
Read the linguistic definition: praise is commendation of a voluntary beautiful act in a veneration manner. Note the condition 'voluntary' to exclude involuntary qualities.
2
Grasp the terminological definition and its three types
Sanusi defines praise as an act indicating veneration of the Benefactor. This act can be speech, belief, or action. Notice that belief is included despite being hidden.
The poetry quote illustrates the three types.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The objection says we cannot know inner belief. The answer: although not directly known, circumstantial evidence reveals it. Understand how this preserves the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحمد لغةً: هو الثناء بالجميل على الجميل الاختياري على جهة التعظيم. واصطلاحاً: فِعْلٌ يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنْعِم بسبب كونه مُنْعِماً على الحامد أو غيره، سواء كان ذلك الفعل قولاً باللسان، أو اعتقاداً بالجنان، أو عملاً بالأركان، كما قيل: أَفَادَتْكُمُ النَّعْمَاءُ مِنِّي ثَلَاثَةٌ يَدِي وَلِسَانِي وَالضَّمِيرَ الْمُحَجَّبَا فإن قيل: لا اطلاع لنا على الاعتقاد حتى يُنبئ عن تعظيم المنعم. أجيب: بأنه وإن كان لا اطلاع لنا عليه؛ لكن تَدلُّنا عليه قرائن الأحوال.
And praise (al-ḥamd) linguistically: it is commendation with beauty upon the voluntary beautiful [act] in a manner of veneration. And terminologically: it is an act that indicates the veneration of the Benefactor due to His being a Benefactor to the one praising or others, whether that act is speech with the tongue, or belief with the heart, or action with the limbs, as was said: 'Your favor from me has bestowed three things: my hand, my tongue, and the veiled conscience.' If it is said: we have no awareness of belief so that it indicates the veneration of the Benefactor. It is answered: that although we have no awareness of it, yet indications of the circumstances indicate it to us.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ḥamd' (praise) both linguistically and terminologically. Sanusi first gives the linguistic definition: it is commendation (thnā’) directed at a beautiful act that is voluntary (ikhtiyārī), done in a manner of veneration. The qualification 'voluntary' restricts the object of praise to acts freely chosen, excluding involuntary attributes like physical beauty. The terminological definition is then given: praise is an act that indicates the veneration of the Benefactor (al-mun‘im) because He is a benefactor to the one praising or to others. This act can be of three types: speech (qawl), inner conviction (i‘tiqād), or physical action (‘amal). Sanusi cites a line of poetry illustrating these three. An objection is then raised: we have no direct knowledge of another's inner conviction, so how can it 'indicate' veneration? The answer explains that while we do not have direct access to the heart, external circumstantial evidence (qarā’in al-aḥwāl) reveals the inner state. Thus the definition holds, as the act of conviction is rendered knowable through its signs.

Key Terms
الحمد
الثناء
الجميل الاختياري
جهة التعظيم
فعل
المنعم
قول
اعتقاد
عمل
قرائن الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praise is only about outward speech or actions, not inner belief.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly includes inner conviction (i‘tiqād) as one of the three forms of praise, and argues it is known through circumstantial evidence.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that we cannot know inner conviction?
A) It is not required for praise.
B) Circumstantial evidence indicates it.
C) It is known through revelation.
D) It is assumed without proof.

Key Takeaway
Praise is a comprehensive act that includes speech, belief, and action, and even hidden belief is known through its signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Alhamdulillah', do you accompany it with a heart fully convinced of Allah's beneficence, or is it merely a habit of the tongue? What one action today can you perform to make your praise more complete?
?
The passage teaches that inner belief is known through external signs. If someone observed your daily life, what signs would they see that indicate your veneration of Allah? Are there any signs that contradict it?
?
Your praise of Allah is an act that should involve your whole being—speech, heart, and limbs. Which of these three is most lacking in your life right now, and what specific step will you take this week to strengthen that dimension of your hamd?

Definition of Shukr and Its Relationship with Hamd
Al-Bajuri defines terminological shukr and states its logical relationship with hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of gratitude (shukr) in Islamic theology and its precise logical relationship with praise (hamd).

Core Idea
True gratitude means using every blessing for its intended purpose—a rare virtue.

This lesson explores the theological definition of shukr (gratitude) as given by Imam al-Bajuri. We will see that true gratitude is an extremely high standard, supported by a Quranic verse, and then clarify how this technical term relates to other related concepts like hamd (praise) in a precise logical framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author’s own words and rhythm come through.
Notice the compact style—the definition is in one sentence, then a Quranic proof, then a logical statement.
2
Understand the definition
The definition of terminological shukr is 'the servant's spending all that God has bestowed upon him in that for which it was created.' Ask yourself: what does this entail? Why might it be nearly impossible?
3
Note the Quranic evidence
The author cites Quran 34:13 to support the rarity of true gratitude. The word 'shakūr' (intensive form) stresses that even the best servants are few. Reflect on how scripture underpins the theological point.
The verse is from Surah Saba’, and the glossator uses the Quranic form 'ash-shakūr'.
4
Grasp the logical relationship
The passage states that the relationship between terminological shukr and each of the three other terms (linguistic hamd, terminological hamd, linguistic shukr) is 'absolute generality and specificity.' This means one term is always broader, the other always narrower. Consider which might be more general.
The passage does not specify which is general and which is specific, only that the relationship is of that type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه صَرْفُ العبد جميع ما أنعم الله به عليه فيما خُلِقَ لأجله وهو، لا يكاد يُوجَدُ، قال الله تعالى: وَقَلِيلٌ مِنْ عِبَادِيَ الشَّكُورُ. واعلم أن النسبة بين الشكر الاصطلاحي وبين كل من الحمد اللغوي والاصطلاحي والشكر اللغوي عموم وخصوص مطلق.
For it is the servant's spending all that God has bestowed upon him in that for which it was created, and it is almost non-existent. God Almighty said: 'And few of My servants are the grateful (ash-shakūr).' And know that the relationship between terminological gratitude (ash-shukr al-iṣṭilāḥī) and each of linguistic praise (al-ḥamd al-lughawī), terminological praise (al-ḥamd al-iṣṭilāḥī), and linguistic gratitude (ash-shukr al-lughawī) is absolute generality and specificity.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage from al-Bajuri's gloss defines terminological gratitude (ash-shukr al-iṣṭilāḥī) and then clarifies its logical relationship with other related terms. The definition: gratitude is the servant's using all of God's blessings for the purpose for which they were created. This is a very high standard, so the author remarks that it is almost never found (lā yakādu yūjad). He cites the Quranic verse (Saba’ 34:13) to support the rarity of true gratitude, using the form 'shakūr' (intensive) to emphasize that even the best of the select are few. Then he states a key logical relationship: between terminological gratitude and each of the three related concepts—linguistic praise, terminological praise, and linguistic gratitude—there is a relationship of absolute generality and specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq). This means that one term is more general in all respects, and the other is more specific in all respects; i.e., the specific term is always included under the general term but not vice versa. The passage thus provides a precise classification of these key Kalam terms.

Key Terms
الشكر الاصطلاحي
الحمد اللغوي
الحمد الاصطلاحي
الشكر اللغوي
عموم وخصوص مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'shukr' and 'ḥamd' are synonyms with no distinction.
Correction: The passage shows they have distinct definitions and a precise logical relationship of absolute generality and specificity.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between terminological shukr and terminological hamd?
A) They are synonyms.
B) They are opposites.
C) Absolute generality and specificity.
D) Partial overlap.

Key Takeaway
Terminological shukr is a precise concept defined as using blessings for their purpose, and it relates to hamd via absolute generality and specificity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one specific blessing in your life—your health, your wealth, your knowledge, or your time. Are you using it for the exact purpose for which Allah gave it to you? If not, what is one concrete change you will make today to align that blessing with its intended purpose? Write it down as a commitment.
?
The passage describes true gratitude as almost nonexistent. Does that description sting you? When you examine your heart, do you find that your gratitude is mostly lip service, or does it actually drive how you use your resources? Ask yourself: if Allah examined every blessing I have received today, would He find me truly thankful in action? What must change?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment, and He asks you about each blessing—how you used it. Will you be among the few who can say they spent it in the way He created it for? What is stopping you from being that person starting now? Repent and resolve to act.

The Three Meanings of Salat
Al-Bajuri classifies the three meanings of salat: linguistic, legal, and combined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the word 'salat': purely linguistic, purely legal, and a combination of both, and how they apply to God and angels.

Core Idea
The word 'salat' has three meanings depending on context: supplication, ritual prayer, and mercy/forgiveness.

Before diving into deeper theological arguments, it's essential to clarify the terminology. The word 'salat' might seem straightforward, but it carries multiple layers of meaning. Al-Bajuri begins by distinguishing its linguistic root from its technical legal usage, and then shows how both combine when referring to God or angels. This classification prevents confusion in later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly. Notice how al-Bajuri enumerates the three meanings with numbering (الأول, الثاني, الثالث).
The passage is short but packed.
2
Understand the first meaning
The purely linguistic meaning is 'supplication' (du'a). Note the minor difference: some restrict it to supplication for good.
3
Understand the second meaning
The purely legal meaning is the ritual prayer with its specific structure: opening takbir, closing taslim, and conditions.
This is what Muslims perform five times a day.
4
Grasp the combined meaning
The third meaning blends both: when 'salat' is attributed to God, it means mercy; when to angels, it means seeking forgiveness. This is the view of the majority.
This shows the term's flexibility.
5
Apply the knowledge
Consider how this classification helps in interpreting Quranic verses where 'salat' appears in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن للصلاة ثلاثة معانٍ: الأول معنى لغوي فقط، وهو الدعاء مطلقاً. وقيل: بخير. والثاني: معنى شرعي فقط، وهو أقوال وأفعال مُفْتَتَحَةٌ بالتكبير مُخْتَتَمَةٌ بالتسليم بشرائط مخصوصة. والثالث لغوي وشرعي، وهو عند الجمهور بالنسبة الله الرحمة، وبالنسبة للملائكة: الاستغفارُ .
Know that prayer (ṣalāh) has three meanings: first, a purely linguistic meaning, which is supplication absolutely. It is said: [supplication] for good. Second, a purely legal meaning, which is words and deeds begun with takbīr and concluded with taslīm, with specific conditions. Third, both linguistic and legal, and according to the majority (jumhūr), in relation to God it is mercy, and in relation to the angels it is seeking forgiveness (al-istighfār).
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsīm). Al-Bajuri states that the word ṣalāh has three distinct meanings. (1) The purely linguistic meaning is simply 'supplication' (duʿā'), whether for good or evil, but some restrict it to supplication for good. (2) The purely legal meaning is the ritual prayer (ṣalāh) with its specific pillars and conditions. (3) The meaning that combines both linguistic and legal aspects is used when ṣalāh is attributed to God (meaning mercy) or to angels (meaning seeking forgiveness). This last is the view of the majority. The passage thus clarifies the semantic range of the term.

Key Terms
الصلاة
المعنى اللغوي
المعنى الشرعي
الدعاء
الاستغفار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think ṣalāh only means the ritual prayer (legal meaning) and nothing else.
Correction: The passage shows ṣalāh has a broader linguistic meaning (supplication) and a combined meaning (mercy/forgiveness) when applied to God or angels.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The word 'salat' has three distinct meanings: linguistic (supplication), legal (ritual prayer), and combined (mercy for God, forgiveness for angels).

The Three Meanings of Salat
Al-Bajuri enumerates the three meanings of salat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the word 'salat': linguistic (supplication), legal (ritual prayer), and combined (varying by subject).

Core Idea
Salat has three meanings depending on context: supplication, ritual prayer, and a combined meaning for Allah, angels, and others.

Al-Bajuri begins by clarifying a crucial semantic point: the word 'salat' is not always used in the same sense. Before we can properly understand theological texts, we must know which meaning is intended. In this passage, he lays out the three standard meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the three meanings
Read the Arabic passage once and note the three categories al-Bajuri presents. Then read the translation to confirm your understanding.
Pay attention to the definitions: linguistic, legal, and combined.
2
Define each meaning in your own words
Write a short definition for each meaning: linguistic (du'a), legal (ritual prayer with takbir and taslim), and combined (mercy for Allah, istighfar for angels, supplication for others).
The combined meaning is context-dependent.
3
Consider why this distinction matters
Reflect on how this semantic clarification helps when interpreting the Quran and hadith. For example, when Allah sends salat upon the Prophet, it means mercy, not ritual prayer.
This prevents confusion in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن للصلاة ثلاثة معانٍ: الأول معنى لغوي فقط، وهو الدعاء مطلقاً. وقيل: بخير. والثاني: معنى شرعي فقط، وهو أقوال وأفعال مُفْتَتَحَةٌ بالتكبير مُخْتَتَمَةٌ بالتسليم بشرائط مخصوصة. والثالث لغوي وشرعي، وهو عند الجمهور بالنسبة الله الرحمة، وبالنسبة للملائكة: الاستغفارُ . وبالنسبة لغيرهم - ولو شَجَراً أو حَجَراً أو مَدَراً - : التضرُّعُ والدعاء لثبوت صلاتها على النبي كما رواه الحَلَبِيُّ في "السيرة "(۱) ؛ وإن اشتهر أنها سَلَّمَتْ عليه فقط. وإن شئتَ قلتَ ـ وهو الأخصرُ - بالنسبة الله الرحمة، وبالنسبة لغيره من الملائكة وغيرهم الدعاءُ، وحينئذ يكون شاملاً للاستغفار وغيره. واختار ابنُ هِشَامٍ في "مغنيه (۲): أنه العطفُ - بفتح العين ، وهو بالنسبة الله الرحمةُ إلخ. ويترتب على هذا الخلاف أنها من قبيل المشترك اللفظي على الأول، وضابطه: أن يتَّحِدَ اللفظ ويتعدد المعنى، كما في لفظ: عين، فإنه واحد ومعناه
And know that al-ṣalāt (the prayer/blessing) has three meanings: The first is a purely linguistic meaning, which is absolute supplication; it is said: with good. The second is a purely legal meaning, which are words and actions begun with takbīr and ended with taslīm, with specific conditions. The third is linguistic and legal, and according to the majority it is, with respect to Allāh, mercy; and with respect to the angels, seeking forgiveness. And with respect to others—even a tree, a stone, or dirt—it is supplication and prayer, due to the confirmation that they sent ṣalāt upon the Prophet as reported by al-Ḥalabī in al-Sīra, even though it is famous that they only greeted him with salām. And if you wish, you may say—and it is the shortest—that it is, with respect to Allāh, mercy, and with respect to others, angels and non-angels, supplication, and in that case it includes seeking forgiveness and other than it. And Ibn Hishām chose in his Mughnī that it is al-‘aṭf (with fatḥa on ‘ayn), and it is, with respect to Allāh, mercy, etc. And it follows from this disagreement that it is of the type of homonym (mushtarak lafẓī) according to the first view; its definition is that the word is one and the meaning is multiple, as in the word ‘ayn, for it is one and its meaning...
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author, al-Bajuri, gives a comprehensive exposition of the meanings of the word 'ṣalāt'. He identifies three: (1) purely linguistic (lughawī) meaning: supplication (du‘ā'), either absolute or specifically for good. (2) purely legal (shar‘ī) meaning: the ritual prayer consisting of specific utterances and actions beginning with takbīr and ending with taslīm under particular conditions. (3) a combined linguistic and legal meaning: according to the majority (jumhūr), when applied to Allāh it means mercy; when applied to angels it means seeking forgiveness; and when applied to other beings—even inanimate objects like trees, stones, or dirt—it means humble supplication (taḍarru‘) and prayer, as recorded in al-Ḥalabī's Sīra that such objects sent ṣalāt upon the Prophet, though it is well-known they only greeted him. The author then offers a shorter formulation: for Allāh, mercy; for others, supplication, which then includes seeking forgiveness and other forms. He notes that Ibn Hishām in his Mughnī chose the view that ṣalāt is 'al-‘aṭf' (with fatḥa on ‘ayn), meaning mercy for Allāh. The passage concludes by explaining a consequence of the disagreement: according to the first view (that ṣalāt has three distinct meanings), it is a homonym (mushtarak lafẓī), defined as a single word with multiple meanings, like the word 'ayn (eye/spring/essence).

Key Terms
صلاة
معنى لغوي
معنى شرعي
الدعاء
الرحمة
الاستغفار
التضرع

Misconception: Salat always refers to ritual prayer.
Correction: The text shows it has multiple meanings including supplication and mercy, depending on context.

Checkpoint

A) Mercy
B) Ritual prayer
C) Seeking forgiveness
D) Supplication

Key Takeaway
The word 'salat' has three distinct meanings: linguistic (supplication), legal (ritual prayer), and combined (mercy, seeking forgiveness, supplication depending on the subject).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Variant Views and the Homonym Nature of Salat
Al-Bajuri presents variant interpretations and concludes that salat is a homonym.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative formulation of the combined meaning, Ibn Hisham's view, and the conclusion that salat is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi).

Core Idea
The word 'salat' is a homonym: one word with multiple distinct meanings.

After presenting the majority view on the combined meaning, al-Bajuri offers a shorter alternative and notes Ibn Hisham's different opinion. This leads to the important linguistic conclusion that 'salat' is a homonym. These details refine our understanding of the word's usage in sacred texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative formulation
Read the shorter definition al-Bajuri suggests: for Allah it is mercy, for others (including angels) it is supplication. Note how this simplifies the classification.
This formulation makes salat include istighfar as a type of supplication.
2
Note Ibn Hisham's view
Ibn Hisham in his Mughni chose that salat is 'al-‘atf' (with fatha on 'ayn), meaning it is mercy for Allah. Understand how this differs from the majority.
The term 'atf here likely refers to a specific linguistic interpretation.
3
Understand the homonym concept
Al-Bajuri explains that according to the first view (that salat has three distinct meanings), the word is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi). Read the definition and example (the word 'ayn).
A homonym is a single word with multiple unrelated meanings.

Alternative View: Salat Has Only Two Meanings
An anonymous view states salat has only two meanings, the third being metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative classification of the meanings of ṣalāt and the reasoning behind considering one meaning metaphorical.

Core Idea
An alternative view restricts ṣalāt to two literal meanings; the third is metaphorical.

While the standard position recognizes three meanings of ṣalāt, an anonymous view simplifies the matter. This view reduces the literal meanings to two—linguistic supplication and legal ritual prayer—and treats the application to Allah as metaphorical. Let's examine this perspective and the principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice the concise style of the argument.
2
Identify the two literal meanings
The passage states that ṣalāt has only two literal meanings: supplication (linguistic) and specific words and actions (legal). Make sure you can locate these.
3
Understand the metaphorical reasoning
The passage explains why applying ṣalāt to Allah as mercy is metaphorical—it cites a principle: what is impossible for Allah in its origin can be attributed to Him in its end. Reflect on this reasoning.
This connects to broader theological principles about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعضهم: ليس للصلاة إلا معنيان فقط: الدعاء، والأقوال والأفعال المخصوصة، الأوّلُ لُغَوِيّ، والثاني شرعي، وأما إطلاقها على الرحمة بالنسبة الله؛ فهو مجاز؛ لأن كل شيء اسْتَحَالَ على الله باعتبار مبدئه؛ جَازَ إطلاقه عليه تعالى باعتبار غايته.
And some said: There is no meaning for al-ṣalāt except two only: supplication, and the specific words and actions; the first is linguistic, the second is legal. As for applying it to mercy with respect to Allāh, it is metaphorical; because everything that is impossible for Allāh in terms of its origin, it is permissible to apply it to Him, exalted, in terms of its end.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative opinion (attributed to 'some') that narrows the meanings of ṣalāt to just two: the linguistic meaning of supplication and the legal meaning of the ritual prayer. According to this view, when ṣalāt is applied to Allāh as mercy, it is not a literal meaning but a metaphorical one (majāz). The justification is a principle: if a concept cannot be attributed to Allāh in its primary sense (e.g., supplication, which implies need), it may still be used metaphorically by considering its ultimate consequence—here, mercy is the outcome of the prayer. This view simplifies the classification and avoids the complications of multiple literal meanings.

Key Terms
صلاة
دعاء
مجاز
رحمة
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All applications of ṣalāt are literal.
Correction: According to this view, when applied to Allah, ṣalāt is metaphorical because its origin (supplication) is impossible for Allah, but its end (mercy) is not.

Checkpoint

B) Metaphorical
C) Both literal and metaphorical
D) It is forbidden

Key Takeaway
According to this view, ṣalāt has only two literal meanings; the application to Allah is metaphorical based on the principle of impossibility of origin.
