Is God's Judgment Temporal? The Ta'alluq Debate
Al-Anbābī discusses the temporality of the relation of God's speech to human acts and defends the eternality of the judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of ta'alluq (relation) of God's speech to human acts and the debate over whether it makes the judgment temporal.

Core Idea
The relation of God's speech to acts is temporal, but the speech itself remains eternal.

In the final part of the passage, al-Anbābī addresses a subtle point: the relation (taʿalluq) of God's eternal speech to temporal human acts. Does this relation arise in time? If so, does that make the judgment temporal? Al-Anbābī explains that the relation is one of indication, not causation, and it is a temporal attribute of the speech, but the speech itself (the judgment) remains eternal. This resolves a key kalām issue.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
The issue: if God's speech is eternal, how can it be 'related' to a temporal act? Relation seems to occur only when the act exists or the command is given.
2
Identify the type of relation
Al-Anbābī specifies that ta'alluq here is a relation of indication (دلالة), not of effect or disclosure. It occurs temporally when the request is directed.
The relation is not a real attribute but a mental consideration.
3
See the conclusion
Because the relation is not a real attribute, its occurrence does not imply that the qualified (God's speech) is temporal. The speech remains eternal.
Al-Maḥallī held the opposite view that the judgment is temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقُ بِفِعْل الشَّخْصِ) أي: الدال عليه.
(قوله: مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّكْلِيف :أي لا من حيث إنه مخلوق أو ممكن مثلاً، ومراده بالتكليف ما يعم الطلب بأقسامه الأربعة والإباحة.
(وقوله: أوِ الْوَضْع لَهُ أي للتكليف الشامل للطلب بأقسامه الأربعة وللإباحة ومعنى الوضع لذلك: جعل الشارع لذلك سبباً أو شرطاً أو مانعاً وإثباته الصحة إن استوفى الأمور المعتبرة فيه والفساد إن لم يستوفها، فالطلب بأقسامه الأربعة والإباحة والوضع بالمعنى المتقدم مدلولة للكلام كما يعلم من "حاشية الهدهدي" وإن كان الكلام نفسه طلباً وإباحة ووضعاً باعتبار دلالته عليها كما يعلم أيضاً من الحاشية المذكورة، والظاهر أن إطلاق الطلب والإباحة والوضع على الكلام باعتبار تلك الدلالة مجازي من إطلاق اسم المدلول على الدال إلا أن يكونوا اصطلحوا على ذلك الإطلاق، فيكون إطلاقاً حقيقياً.
(قوله: (الْمُتَعَلَّقُ) أي: تعلّق دِلالةٍ، لا تأثير ولا انكشاف، والمراد: تعلقاً تنجيزياً حادثاً عند توجه الطلب، ولا يلزم من حدوث التعلق الذي هو صِفَةُ الكلام حدوث الحُكْمِ المفسر بالكلام المذكور؛ لأن التعلُّق المذكور ليس صِفَةً حقيقة، بل هو نِسْبَةٌ واعتبار، فلا يلزم من حدوثها حدوث موصوفها، فالحكم قديم لا حادث، وذهب العلامة المَحَلِّي إلى حدوثه.
And the third: the legal judgment: it is the speech of God related to the action of a person in terms of obligation or stipulation for it. (His saying: 'And it is the speech of God... etc.') If it is said: 'The speech of God is not affirmation of a matter for a matter... etc.,' then the answer is with one of two things: the first: that it is affirmation in consideration of its necessary consequence, because the speech of God that necessitated the request in a decisive manner includes our affirmation of obligation. Similarly, the second: that the definition of the legal judgment as mentioned is a terminology of the uṣūliyyūn, and the terminology of the fuqahā' is that the legal judgment is the affirmation of a matter for a matter or the negation of it from it by means of a legal proof. (His saying: 'related to the action of a person') i.e., indicating it. (His saying: 'in terms of obligation') i.e., not in terms of it being created or possible, for example, and his intention by obligation is what includes the request in its four divisions and permission. (And his saying: 'or stipulation for it') i.e., for the obligation that includes the request in its four divisions and permission; and the meaning of stipulation for that is: the Lawgiver's making for that a cause, or a condition, or an impediment, and His affirming its validity if it fulfills the considerations and its invalidity if it does not fulfill them. So the request in its four divisions, permission, and stipulation in the aforementioned sense are the meanings indicated by the speech, as is known from 'Hashiyat al-Hudhdhī', even if the speech itself is request, permission, and stipulation in consideration of its indication of them, as is also known from the aforementioned hashya. And the apparent is that the application of request, permission, and stipulation to speech in consideration of that indication is metaphorical, from applying the name of the indicated to the indicator, unless they have made it a conventional application, in which case it would be literal. (His saying: 'the related') i.e., a relation of indication, not of effect nor of disclosure, and what is meant is an actualized relation that occurs when the request is directed; and the occurrence of the relation, which is an attribute of speech, does not necessitate the occurrence of the judgment interpreted as the mentioned speech, because the mentioned relation is not a real attribute, but rather a relation and consideration, so its occurrence does not necessitate the occurrence of its qualified; thus the judgment is eternal, not temporal, and al-ʿAllāma al-Maḥallī went to its temporality.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines legal judgment (al-ḥukm al-sharʿī) and discusses several important points. Sanusi defines it as God's speech (kalām Allāh) relating to a person's act in terms of taklīf (obligation) or waḍʿ (stipulation). The commentary first addresses a potential objection: God's speech is not itself an affirmation or negation (as in the rational judgment), so how can it be a 'judgment'? Two answers are given: (1) God's speech that commands or prohibits necessarily implies our affirmation of obligation, so it is affirmation by necessary consequence; (2) jurists (fuqahā') define legal judgment differently—as affirmation or negation by a legal proof, which is a different convention. Then al-Ajhuri explains that 'related to the action of a person' means indicating it, and 'in terms of obligation' excludes considering the act as mere creation or possibility. Obligation here includes the four types of request (command, prohibition, etc.) and permission (ibāḥa). 'Stipulation' means the Lawgiver designates something as a cause, condition, or impediment, and declares validity or invalidity. The commentary notes that the terms 'request', 'permission', and 'stipulation' are applied to God's speech metaphorically in terms of their indication, unless conventionalized. Finally, al-Anbābī's gloss explains that the relation (taʿalluq) of God's speech to human acts is a relation of indication, not of causation; it occurs temporally when the request is directed, but this does not entail that the judgment itself (which is the speech) is temporal, because the relation is not a real attribute. Therefore, the judgment (God's speech) is eternal, though al-Maḥallī held it to be temporal. This touches on the kalām debate about the eternality of God's speech.

Key Terms

دلالة
تأثير
انكشاف
تنجيزي
حكم
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if the relation of God's speech to acts occurs in time, then God's speech itself must be temporal.
Correction: The relation is not a real attribute but a consideration, so the speech remains eternal.

Checkpoint
What type of relation does al-Anbābī say ta'alluq is?
A) Relation of effect
B) Relation of disclosure
C) Relation of indication
D) Relation of causation

Key Takeaway
The relation of God's speech to acts is temporal but does not compromise the eternality of the speech because it is not a real attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's judgment is eternal and relates to your actions only in a temporal, indicative way, how does that affect your understanding of divine decree? Are you living as if your choices carry eternal weight, or do you treat them as merely temporal?
?
Al-Maḥallī held that the judgment is temporal. Do you ever find yourself treating God's commands as if they are outdated or contingent on circumstances? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed every divine command is eternal and rooted in His timeless wisdom?
?
The relation of God's speech to your acts is described as a mere 'consideration' (iʿtibār). Does this challenge your assumption that God is directly engaged with every moment? How can this deepen your reliance on His eternal nature rather than on your perception of His involvement?

The Three Rational Judgments and Definition of Intellect
Al-Bajuri defines the three rational judgments and the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories (necessary, impossible, possible) and the definition of intellect as a spiritual light that is an instrument of the soul.

Core Idea
Intellect is not the perceiver; the soul perceives through it.

In kalam, before discussing Allah's attributes, we must establish the basic rational categories. This lesson begins with the necessary, impossible, and possible. It then presents the best definition of intellect as a spiritual light by which the soul perceives knowledge, clarifying that the intellect is merely an instrument.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational judgments
Begin by reading the Arabic passage on the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible. Notice how each is defined in terms of accepting existence or non-existence.
2
Identify the definition of intellect
Next, find the best definition of intellect: a spiritual light by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical knowledge. Note that many definitions existed, but this is the best.
3
Understand the soul as real perceiver
The commentary explains that perception belongs to the soul, not the intellect. The intellect is only an instrument, like a knife for cutting. Reflect on the analogy.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the word 'fi'
The text shows that the word 'fi' (in) in the definition indicates instrumentality, not location. Consider how this linguistic point reinforces the instrumental role of intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب ما لا يقبل الانتفاء، والمستحيل : ما لا يقبل الثبوت، والجائز: ما يقبلهما. وقد وقع لهم في حدّ العقل تعاريف كثيرةً، أحسنها: أنه نور روحاني به تدرك النفس العلوم الضرورية والنظرية واستفيد من هذا التعريف: أن المدرك في الحقيقة هي النفس، وإنما العقل آلة في الإدراك كسائر القوى، ولذلك قال ابن قاسم في 'آياته': اتفق المحققون على أن المدرك للكليات والجزئيات هي النفس الناطقة، وأن نسبة الإدراك إلى قواها كنسبة القطع إلى السكين اهـ. وبهذا كله ظهر أن (في) هنا سببية. فتأمل. (قوله نُورُ) أي آلةٌ في إدراك المعاني، كما أن النور الحسي آلة في إدراك المحسوسات فإطلاق النور عليه من باب الاستعارة. (قوله: رُوحاني) نسبة إلى الروح لكونه صفة لها (قوله: كَسَائِرِ الْقُوَى هي آلاتُ الإدراك وهي الحس المشترك، وخزانته هي التي في الخيال، وهما في التجويف الأول من الرأس، والمتصرفة، ويقال لها: المفكرة، وهي في التجويف الأوسط من الرأس والواهمة والحافظة، وهما في التجويف الأخير من الرأس.
The necessary is that which does not accept non-existence, and the impossible is that which does not accept existence, and the possible is that which accepts both. And they have many definitions for the definition of intellect, the best of which is: it is a spiritual light by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. And from this definition it is understood that the perceiver in reality is the soul, and that the intellect is only an instrument in perception like the other faculties. Therefore, Ibn Qasim said in his 'Ayat': The verified scholars agreed that the perceiver of universals and particulars is the rational soul, and that the attribution of perception to its powers is like the attribution of cutting to the knife. End. And by all this it became clear that 'in' (fi) here is causal (sababiyya). So reflect. (His statement: 'light') meaning an instrument in perceiving meanings, just as sensory light is an instrument in perceiving sensed things, so calling it light is metaphorical. (His statement: 'spiritual') relative to the spirit because it is an attribute of it. (His statement: 'like the rest of the powers') they are the instruments of perception: the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), its storehouse is the faculty of imagination (al-khayal), and they are in the first cavity of the head; the thinking faculty (al-mutasarifa), also called the cogitative faculty (al-mufakkira), is in the middle cavity; the estimative faculty (al-wahima) and the memory (al-hafiza) are in the last cavity.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage begins with the standard definitions of the three rational judgments: necessary (wajib) is that which cannot be non-existent; impossible (mustahil) is that which cannot be existent; possible (ja'iz) is that which can be both. It then moves to the definition of intellect (aql). Many definitions were given, but the best is that intellect is a spiritual light (nur ruhani) by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical knowledge. From this definition, it is deduced that the real perceiver is the soul, and the intellect is merely an instrument like the other faculties. The commentator cites Ibn Qasim, who states that the rational soul perceives both universals and particulars, and the relation of perception to its powers is like cutting to a knife. This shows that the 'in' (fi) in the definition is causal (sababiyya), indicating the instrument. The commentator then explains the components: 'light' means an instrument for perceiving meanings, metaphorically called light. 'Spiritual' relates to the spirit. 'Like the rest of the powers' refers to the various internal faculties of perception: common sense (hiss mushtarak), imagination (khayal) in the first cavity of the brain; the thinking or cogitative faculty (mutasarifa/mufakkira) in the middle cavity; and the estimative faculty (wahima) and memory (hafiza) in the last cavity. This anatomical localization was common in medieval Islamic psychology.

Key Terms
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
عقل
نور روحاني
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the intellect perceives directly without the soul.
Correction: The passage clarifies that perception belongs to the soul, and the intellect is merely an instrument, like a knife for cutting.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best definition of intellect (aql)?
A) It is a light in the brain
B) It is a spiritual light by which the soul perceives knowledge
C) It is a faculty of the heart
D) It is a collection of internal senses

Key Takeaway
The three rational categories (necessary, impossible, possible) and intellect as a spiritual light instrumentally used by the soul are foundational for kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your intellect is merely an instrument, not the true knower. The soul perceives, and the intellect is like a knife that cuts. When you rely on your intelligence alone, do you feel a sense of pride or self-sufficiency? How will you remind yourself daily that your intellectual gifts are tools given by Allah, and that humility before Him is the only proper response?
?
The three categories—necessary, impossible, and possible—apply to everything. Think of one belief you hold about Allah. Is it necessary (must be true), impossible (cannot be true), or possible (could be otherwise)? Now be honest: have you ever treated a possible matter as if it were necessary, causing you to judge others or despair? How can you use this lesson to bring more balance and trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
The scholar Ibn Qasim compared perception to cutting with a knife. You own a knife, but it cuts nothing without your hand. Similarly, your intellect produces nothing without the soul's command from Allah. Right now, consider a time when you were proud of a smart conclusion you reached. Did you thank Allah for enabling your soul to perceive through your intellect? Will you now begin to see every act of understanding as a direct gift from Him, and live in constant gratitude?

The Five Internal Faculties of Perception
Al-Bajuri enumerates the five internal faculties of perception and their brain locations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five internal senses (common sense, imagination, cogitation, estimation, memory) and their locations in the three brain cavities.

Core Idea
The five inner senses are instruments of the soul, each with a specific brain location.

After defining intellect, al-Bajuri enumerates the internal faculties through which the soul perceives. These five faculties are located in three cavities of the brain, each with a specific function. This medieval psychological model helps us understand how the soul uses these instruments.

Guided Path
1
Read the faculty list
Read the Arabic passage that lists the internal faculties. Notice the names: common sense, imagination, cogitative, estimative, and memory.
2
Identify the brain cavities
The faculties are grouped into three brain cavities: front (common sense and imagination), middle (cogitative), back (estimative and memory). Locate each group.
3
Understand the pairing
Common sense and imagination are paired together in the front cavity. Estimation and memory are paired in the back. The cogitative faculty is alone in the middle.
4
Connect to the soul
Recall that these faculties are instruments (آلات) of the soul. The soul uses them to perceive, just as a knife is used to cut. Reflect on how this relates to the definition of intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله نُورُ) أي آلةٌ في إدراك المعاني، كما أن النور الحسي آلة في إدراك المحسوسات فإطلاق النور عليه من باب الاستعارة. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your estimative faculty constantly judges things as good or bad, safe or dangerous. Think of a recent situation where you acted on an immediate estimation (wahm) rather than pausing to reflect with your intellect. What was the outcome? How might you train your inner faculties to align with the truth rather than impulsive judgments?
?
Memory (al-hafiza) stores your past judgments and experiences. What negative assumptions about others or about Allah's decree are stored in your memory that contradict tawakkul? Will you actively replace them with the remembrance of His mercy and wisdom?
?
You perceive the world every moment through these five inner faculties, yet how often do you consider that they are instruments of your soul? If the soul's purpose is to know Allah, how can you use these faculties more intentionally to gain deeper knowledge of Him rather than being distracted by worldly perceptions?

Definition of the Intellect as Spiritual Light
Sanusi defines the intellect and explains its role and faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of the intellect as a spiritual light, its instrumental role, and the internal faculties of perception.

Core Idea
The intellect is a spiritual instrument; the soul perceives through it.

In this lesson, we explore Sanusi's definition of the intellect as a spiritual light. He clarifies that the true perceiver is the soul, and the intellect is like a tool. We also learn about the five internal faculties and their locations in the brain.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Start by reading the Arabic and translation of Sanusi's definition of the intellect. Notice the key terms.
The definition is concise yet profound.
2
Understand the metaphor
The intellect is called a 'light' metaphorically. Just as physical light enables sight, the intellect enables the soul to perceive meanings.
This metaphor is common in Islamic philosophy.
3
Grasp the implication
From the definition, Sanusi derives that the soul is the true perceiver. The intellect is merely an instrument, like a knife for cutting.
This distinguishes the intellect from the soul.
4
Identify the faculties
The passage lists five internal faculties: common sense, imagination, cogitative, estimative, and memory. Note their locations in the brain cavities.
These are instruments of the soul for different types of perception.
5
Reflect on causality
The particle 'bi' (by) in the definition indicates causality. The intellect is the cause or means by which the soul perceives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد وَقَعَ لهم في حدّ العقل تعاريف كثيرةً، أحسنها: أنه نور روحاني به تُدْرِكُ النفس العلوم الضرورية والنظرية واستفيد من هذا التعريف: أن المُدْرِكَ في الحقيقة هي النفس، وإنما العقلُ آلَةً في الإدراك كسائر القُوَى، ولذلك قال ابن قاسم في "آياته": اتَّفَقَ المحققون على أن المُدْرِكَ للكليات والجزئيات هي النفس الناطقة، وأن نسبة الإدراك إلى قواها كنسبة القطع إلى السكين اهـ. وبهذا كُلَّه ظَهَرَ أن (في) هنا سببية. فتأمل. (قوله نُورُ) أي آلةٌ في إدراك المعاني، كما أن النور الحسي آلة في إدراك المحسوسات فإطلاق النور عليه من باب الاستعارة. (قوله: رُوحاني) نسبة إلى الروح لكونه صفة لها (قوله: كَسَائِرِ الْقُوَى هى آلاتُ الإدراك وهي الحش المشترك، وخزانته هي التي في الخيال، وهما في التجويف الأول من الرأس، والمتصرفة، ويقال لها: المفكرة، وهي في التجويف الأوسط من الرأس والواهمة والحافظة، وهما في التجويف الأخير من الرأس.
And there occurred to them many definitions of the intellect, the best of which is that it is a spiritual light by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. And it is derived from this definition that the true perceiver is the soul, and that the intellect is only an instrument in perception like the other faculties. Therefore Ibn Qasim said in his Ayat: The verified scholars agree that the perceiver of universals and particulars is the rational soul, and that the relation of perception to its faculties is like the relation of cutting to the knife. End. And by all this it becomes clear that (in) here indicates causality. So reflect. (His saying: 'light') i.e., an instrument in perceiving meanings, just as sensible light is an instrument in perceiving sensible objects, so calling it light is metaphorical. (His saying: 'spiritual') attributed to the soul (ruh) because it is a attribute of it. (His saying: 'like the other faculties') they are instruments of perception: the common sense and its treasury which is in the imagination, both in the first cavity of the head; the cogitative, also called the thinking faculty, in the middle cavity; and the estimative and memory, both in the last cavity of the head.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines the intellect (al-'aql) and explains its function. Sanusi states that there are many definitions, but the best is that it is a spiritual light (nur ruhani) by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical knowledge. The commentator al-Ajhuri clarifies that 'light' is metaphorical, meaning an instrument for perceiving meanings, just as physical light is an instrument for perceiving physical objects. 'Spiritual' is attributed to the soul because it is a quality of the soul. The passage then explains the implication of this definition: the true perceiver is the soul, and the intellect is merely an instrument among other faculties. Ibn Qasim is quoted confirming that the perceiver of both universals and particulars is the rational soul (al-nafs al-natiqa), and the relationship of perception to its faculties is like the relationship of cutting to the knife—the knife is the instrument, but the cutting action originates from the user. Finally, it is concluded that the particle 'bi' (in 'bihi') in the definition indicates causality. Al-Ajhuri adds a gloss detailing the five internal faculties: common sense, imagination, cogitative, estimative, and memory, and their locations in the brain.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defines the intellect as a spiritual light by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical knowledge, emphasizing that the soul is the true perceiver and the intellect an instrument.

First Objection to Defining the Impossible and the Answer
Sanusi's commentator refutes the claim that the impossible is sometimes conceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossible is defined as 'what is not perceived with assent' rather than 'what is not mentally represented.'

Core Idea
The impossible is not what the mind cannot picture—it is what the mind cannot accept as true.

This passage addresses a crucial objection to the definition of the impossible. A critic argues that we can conceive impossibilities like a partner to God, so the definition seems false. Sanusi's commentator clarifies that the term 'perception' (tasawwur) in the definition actually means 'assent' (tasdiq)—that is, intellectual acceptance—not mere mental imagery. This distinction preserves the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: an objection is raised, then an answer is given.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the answer: the commentator says 'perception' here means 'assent' (tasdiq), not mere conception. Understand why this distinction is critical for the definition.
The objection assumed tasawwur meant mental imagery; the answer redefines it as intellectual acceptance.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the two types of impossible: necessary (e.g., a body devoid of motion and rest) and theoretical (e.g., a partner to Allah). See how each fits the definition.
Necessary impossibles are inherently self-contradictory; theoretical ones require reasoning to reject.
4
Connect to broader framework
The passage reminds that the three rational judgments (necessary, possible, impossible) apply to the impossible just as they do to other categories. Reflect on how this classification helps in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'meaning submission and acceptance' - what is in it has preceded.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the definition of the impossible (al-mustahīl) as 'what is not conceived in the mind to exist'. The objector argues that the mind can indeed conceive the impossible, as previously shown. The answer is that 'perception' (tasawwur) here means 'assent' (tasdīq), i.e., intellectual submission and acceptance, not mere mental representation. Therefore, the impossible is that which the mind cannot accept as existing. The passage then notes that the definition covers both necessary impossibilities (like a body being devoid of motion and rest) and theoretical ones (like a partner to God). The three types of rational judgment (necessary, possible, impossible) apply to the impossible as well. The commentator adds an example: one can say 'if a partner existed, such and such would follow' without actually assenting to the existence of that partner.

Resolving the 'In the Mind' Objection
Sanusi's commentator resolves the objection about 'in the mind' limiting the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'in the mind' in the definition of the impossible is defended against the objection that some people conceive impossibilities in their minds.

Core Idea
The definition of impossibility is about sound rational judgment, not just any thought.

Sanusi's definition of the impossible includes the phrase 'in the mind,' which might seem problematic because people sometimes conceive impossibilities, like the Jewish belief that Ezra is the son of God. How does Sanusi's commentator defend the definition? This passage shows that the definite article in 'al-ʿaql' is interpreted while disregarding preventing relations—those factors that corrupt judgment. Thus, corrupt beliefs do not undermine the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection is raised and then resolved through a careful interpretation of the definite article.
The passage is dense; read it twice to get the flow.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that some minds conceive impossibilities, such as the Jewish belief about Ezra. Understand why this seems to challenge the definition of the impossible.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentator explains that the definite article in 'al-ʿaql' can be for specific reference or generality, but in either case, we disregard 'preventing relations'—factors that hinder correct reasoning. This saves the definition.
4
Connect to broader lesson
Reflect on how this defense maintains the rigor of theological definitions. The definition of the impossible is not about any mental occurrence but about sound rational capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: فِي الْعَقْل) تقدَّمَ أن (أل) فيه إما للعهد أو للاستغراق، لكن بقطع النظر عن العلائق المانعة، فاندفع بذلك ما قد يقال : إنه قد يُتصوَّرُ في بعض العقول وجودُ بعض المستحيلات، فلا تَغْفَلْ (قوله: قَدْ يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي بَعْضِ الْعُقُولِ... إلخ) كاعتقاد اليهود أن عزيراً ابن الله.
His saying: 'in the mind' – it has preceded that the definite article (al) in it is either for specific reference or for generality, but disregarding preventing relations. Thus is repelled what might be said: that in some minds the existence of some impossibilities is conceived. So do not be heedless. His saying: 'it may be conceived in some minds... etc.' like the belief of the Jews that Ezra is the son of God.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding the phrase 'in the mind' in the definition of the impossible. The objector notes that some people, like Jews believing Ezra is the son of God, conceive impossibilities in their minds. The answer is that the definite article 'al' in 'al-ʿaql' can be interpreted either as referring to a specific mind (the sound mind) or generically (every mind), but in either case, it is understood while disregarding preventing relations (i.e., factors that hinder proper judgment). Thus, the definition holds: the impossible is not conceived in the sound mind with proper reasoning, even if it appears in corrupt beliefs.

Key Terms
العقل
العهد
الاستغراق
العلائق المانعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if anyone conceives an impossibility in their mind, then the definition is invalid.
Correction: The definition refers to sound rational judgment disregarding corrupting factors; not every mental occurrence counts.

Checkpoint

D) By redefining impossibility

Key Takeaway
The definition of the impossible is safeguarded by interpreting 'in the mind' as referring to sound rational judgment, free from corrupting influences.

The Objection on the Restriction to Existence and Its Answer
Sanusi's commentator addresses an objection about the term 'existence' in the definition of the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'existence' in the definition of the impossible is interpreted as absolute affirmation, and how this answers the objection about negative attributes and states.

Core Idea
Existence in kalam means absolute affirmation, not just external existence.

In defining the impossible as 'that whose existence is not conceivable,' a problem arises: negative attributes (like blindness) and states (like potentialities) are not external existents, so would they be included? Sanusi's commentator clarifies that 'existence' here means absolute affirmation, which covers both external and mental reality. Let's examine the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the gist of the objection and answer.
The Arabic has some OCR uncertainties; focus on the main argument.
2
Understand the objection
Identify why restricting 'existence' to external existence makes the definition non-exclusive: negative attributes and states are not external existents, so they would be included incorrectly.
Negative attributes (صفات سلبية) are privations like blindness; states (أحوال) are like potentialities.
3
Grasp the answer
Note that 'existence' in the definition means absolute affirmation (مطلق الثبوت), which includes mental existence. Since negative attributes and states have no affirmation even mentally, they are not impossible.
Thus the definition remains exclusive to things that are truly inconceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وُجُودُهُ الضمير عائد على (ما) باعتبار الأفراد نظير ما مر. ويُحِثَ في التقييد بالوجود بأنه يَصِيرُ التعريف غير مانع لدخول كل من صفات الشلُوبِ والأحوالِ فيه؛ لأنه لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده، فإنه ليس من الموجودات. وأجيب: بأن المراد بالوجود مُطلَقُ الثبوت، وحينئذ لا يَردُ ذلك؛ لأنه لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل ثبوته. فتأمل.
His saying: 'its existence' – the pronoun refers back to 'what' considering individuals, analogous to what passed. And it is urged against the restriction to existence that the definition becomes non-exclusive because it includes all negative attributes and states; for their existence is not conceived in the mind, since they are not existents. And it was answered: that what is meant by existence is absolute affirmation (thubūt), and then that does not apply; for their affirmation is not conceived in the mind. So reflect.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the use of the word 'existence' (wujūd) in the definition of the impossible. The objector argues that if existence is restricted to external existence, then the definition would include negative attributes and states (like 'blindness' as a deprivation), since they do not have existence in the mind. The answer is that 'existence' here means 'absolute affirmation' (thubūt), which encompasses both external and mental reality. Thus, the impossible is that whose affirmation (even mentally) is not conceivable. Negative attributes and states, being privations, have no affirmation, so they are not problematic.

Correction: In kalam, الوجود can mean absolute affirmation (مطلق الثبوت), which includes both external and mental existence.

The Pronoun in the Definition of the Impossible: Individuals vs. Essence
Al-Bajuri and al-Ajhuri debate the pronoun's reference in the definition of the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether the pronoun in the definition of the impossible refers to individual instances or the essence, and why this distinction matters for making correct theological judgments.

Core Idea
A single pronoun can change the entire meaning of a theological definition.

In this lesson, we examine a detailed debate between al-Bajuri and al-Ajhuri over a single pronoun in Sanusi's definition of the impossible. At first glance, it may seem like a trivial grammatical point, but the correct interpretation is crucial for accurately judging what is impossible for Allah. Let's follow the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the debate.
Notice that the passage contains an objection, an answer, and a final resolution.
2
Identify the original objection
Find the statement that triggered the debate: the objection that the definition of the impossible seems to apply to the essence itself. Understand why this is problematic.
The objector thought the definition's judgment (non-conception of existence) applies to the composite essence, not just individuals.
3
Analyze al-Ajhuri's initial answer
Al-Ajhuri answers that the pronoun refers to individuals, not the essence. He supports this with three arguments. Identify each argument and see how it defends the definition.
The three arguments involve: (1) the author did not judge the essence, (2) existence means external existence, (3) the definition is designed to be comprehensive and exclusive.
4
Examine the counter-critique and final position
The passage then criticizes al-Ajhuri's own answer, showing it leads to a problem with statements like 'deafness is impossible for God.' The final position is that the pronoun refers back to 'what' in itself, rendering the objection moot.
This shows that even scholarly answers can be refined through rigorous debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. وحاصل الجواب: أن الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد، فالمحكوم عليه بأنه لا يتصور في العقل وجوده أفراد المستحيل، لا حقيقته، وهذا السؤال لا يرد إلا لو قال المصنف والمستحيل: لا يتصور في العقل وجوده مع أنه لم يقل ذلك، وإنما عرفه وبين أن حقيقته شيء لا يتصور وجوده، فجعل عدم تصور الوجود جزءاً من حقيقته مميزاً لها عن حقيقة الواجب والجائز، ولم يحكم على هذه الحقيقة المركبة من الجنس المعبر عنه بـ (ما) والفصل الذي هو عدم تصور الوجود بأنها لا يتصور وجودها، وإنما جعل عدم تصور الوجود فصلاً لها مميزاً لها عن حقيقة الواجب والجائز، فلا وجه لهذا الإيراد. وأيضاً الوجود المذكور في التعريف المراد به الوجود الخارجي لا الذهني، فلا يرد على التعريف إلا ما يتصور في العقل وجوده خارجاً، وحقيقة المستحيل لا يتصور في العقل إلا وجودها ذهناً، وأيضاً فمدار التعريف على أن يكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف مانعاً من دخول غيرها، وهذا التعريف كذلك. فتحصل أن هذا الإيراد ممنوع من جهات ثلاث. وما أجاب به عنه من أن الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد يصير المعنى عليه: أن المستحيل أمر لا يتصور في العقل وجود أفراده، وهذا المعنى يؤدي إلى بطلان الإخبار في قولنا: الصمم مستحيل على الله؛ إذ يصير المعنى عليه الصمم أمر لا يتصور في العقل وجود أفراده، مع أن المقصود : الحكم عليه بأنه لا يتصور وجوده. فالحق أن لا إيراد ولا جواب والضمير عائد على (ما) باعتبار نفسها.
The gloss of the distinguished scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri. And the summary of the answer: that the pronoun refers back to 'what' considering individuals, so that which is judged as not conceivable in the mind with respect to existence are the individual instances of the impossible, not its essence. And this question only arises if the author had said 'the impossible is not conceived in the mind's existence', but he did not say that. Rather, he defined it and explained that its essence is something whose existence is not conceived, thus making non-conception of existence a part of its essence, distinguishing it from the essences of the necessary and the possible. He did not judge this composite essence (composed of the genus expressed by 'what' and the differentia which is non-conception of existence) as being not conceivable in existence. Rather, he made non-conception of existence its differentia distinguishing it from the essences of the necessary and the possible, so there is no basis for this objection. Also, the existence mentioned in the definition is meant as external existence, not mental existence, so nothing enters the definition except what may be conceived in the mind as having external existence; the essence of the impossible is only conceived in the mind mentally. Also, the definition's purpose is to be comprehensive of all individuals of the defined term and exclusive of others, and this definition is so. Thus, this objection is refuted from three angles. And what he answered with, that the pronoun refers to 'what' considering individuals, makes the meaning: 'the impossible is something whose individual instances' existence is not conceived in the mind.' This meaning leads to the falsity of the statement 'deafness is impossible for God', because it would then mean 'deafness is something whose individual instances' existence is not conceived in the mind', whereas the intended meaning is to judge that its existence is not conceived. So the truth is that there is no objection and no answer, and the pronoun refers back to 'what' considered in itself.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a detailed discussion about the pronoun in the definition of the impossible. Al-Ajhuri summarizes the answer: the pronoun refers to the individual instances (afrād) of the impossible, not to its essence. Thus, the definition means that no individual instance of the impossible is conceivable in the mind's existence. The objector mistakenly thought the definition applied to the essence. Then al-Bajuri (or al-Ajhuri) provides three arguments to reject the original objection: (1) The author (Sanusi) did not make a judgment about the composite essence; he only defined it using non-conception as a differentia. (2) The existence in the definition is external existence; the essence of the impossible is only conceivable mentally. (3) The definition is intentionally comprehensive for all individuals and exclusive. However, the text then critiques the answer that the pronoun refers to individuals, showing that it leads to a problem in statements like 'deafness is impossible for God', because it would then mean the individuals of deafness are not conceivable, not that deafness itself is impossible. The final position is that there is no real objection and no answer needed; the pronoun simply refers back to 'what' (mā) in itself.

Key Terms
الضمير
الأفراد
الحقيقة
الوجود الخارجي
الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of the impossible applies to its essence, making a judgment about the essence itself.
Correction: The definition actually refers to individual instances, not the essence, and the judgment is about the non-conception of their existence externally.

Checkpoint
What problem arises if the pronoun in the impossible-definition is explained as referring to individuals?
A) It makes the definition too narrow
B) It invalidates statements like 'deafness is impossible for God'
C) It makes the definition circular
D) It contradicts the earlier answer

Key Takeaway
The pronoun in Sanusi's definition of the impossible ultimately refers back to 'what' in itself, making the definition self-consistent without needing to specify individuals or essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain that a single word in a definition didn't matter, only to discover it changed everything? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own assumptions about seemingly small details in 'Aqeedah?
?
If the definition of the impossible were misunderstood, what would that mean for your understanding of what can and cannot be attributed to Allah? Are you willing to examine your own understanding of divine attributes with this level of precision?
?
The scholars debated the pronoun's reference without dismissing the question as trivial. What does this teach you about the level of care your own theological convictions require? Will you commit to studying 'Aqeedah with this same depth?

The Objection to the Definition of the Possible Regarding States and Considerations
Sanusi's commentator defends the definition of the possible against an objection about states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of the possible (al-jā'iz) was objected to concerning states and contingent considerations, and how the commentator defends its comprehensiveness.

Core Idea
The possible includes states and considerations, even though they are not independent existents.

Sanusi defined the possible as 'what is valid in the mind to exist and not exist.' But an objector argued this excludes states and contingent considerations, which are not existents. The commentator shows how the definition still covers them, using examples from everyday attributes and relations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition and objection
Read the passage's opening lines defining al-jā'iz and the objection that it fails to include states and contingent considerations.
The objection hinges on these entities not being 'existent' in the usual sense.
2
Examine the examples of states
Look at the examples of contingent states, such as Zayd being white or knowledgeable. Notice how these are attributes necessarily following an existent.
States are not independent but are valid in the mind.
3
Examine the examples of considerations
Look at the examples of contingent considerations, such as the subsistence of knowledge in Zayd. Contrast with the eternal consideration of God's power subsistence.
Considerations are relational entities that can be contingent or eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْجَائِزُ) هو والممكنُ بمعنى واحد، فهما مترادفان. (قوله: مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ... إلخ) اعتُرِضَ: بأن هذا التعريف غيرُ جامع لعدم شُمُوله لكلّ من الأحوال والاعتبارات الحادثة؛ لأنه لا يَصِحُ في العقل وجوده وعدمه، فإنه ليس من الموجودات كما تقدم. (قوله: (الْحَادِثَةِ صفةٌ لكل من الأحوال والاعتبارات، فالأحوال الحادثة ككون زيد أبيض اللازم لبياضه، وكون زيد عالماً اللازم لعلمه ومثال الاعتبارات الحادثة: قيام العلم بزيد، فإنه أمر اعتباري حادث، كما أن قيام القدرة بذات الله أمر اعتباري إلا أنه قديم. تقريرات العلامة محمد الألماني (قوله: لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْأَحْوَالِ وَالْاِعْتِبَارَاتِ الْحَادِثَةِ ككون زيد قائماً، وكصحة زيد. وسيأتي الكلام على الأحوال والاعتبارات.
His saying: 'and the possible (al-jā'iz)' – it and 'possible (al-mumkin)' are synonymous. His saying: 'what is valid in the mind... etc.' – it was objected: that this definition is not comprehensive because it does not include every one of the states and contingent considerations; for their existence and non-existence are not valid in the mind, since they are not existents as previously. His saying: 'the contingent (al-ḥāditha)' is an adjective for each of the states and considerations. The contingent states like Zayd being white, necessarily following his whiteness, and Zayd being knowing, necessarily following his knowledge. An example of contingent considerations: the subsistence of knowledge in Zayd, for it is a contingent consideration, just as the subsistence of power in God's essence is a consideration except that it is eternal. (Reports of the distinguished scholar Muhammad al-Almani) His saying: 'for every one of the states and contingent considerations, such as Zayd being standing, and Zayd's health.' And the discussion on states and considerations will come later.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines the possible (al-jā'iz) as synonymous with the possible (al-mumkin). The definition is 'what is valid in the mind to exist and not exist'. An objection is raised: this definition does not include states (aḥwāl) and contingent considerations (iʿtibārāt ḥāditha) because they are not existents, so their existence and non-existence are not valid in the mind. The commentator then explains that the term 'contingent' in the definition is an adjective for states and considerations. Examples are given: states like Zayd being white (contingent on his whiteness) or being knowing (contingent on his knowledge); considerations like the subsistence of knowledge in Zayd (a contingent consideration) versus the subsistence of power in God (an eternal consideration). The passage ends by noting that further discussion on states and considerations will come later.

Key Terms
الجائز
الممكن
الأحوال
الاعتبارات الحادثة
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of the possible excludes states and contingent considerations because they are not independent existents.
Correction: The definition still includes them because they are valid in the mind as contingent attributes or considerations, even if not independent existents.

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of 'al-jā'iz' objected as not comprehensive?
A) It excludes contingent states and considerations
B) It includes impossible things
C) It is limited to necessary existents
D) It is contradictory

Key Takeaway
The definition of the possible encompasses even contingent states and considerations because they are valid in the mind, not just independent existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge, your strength, your very existence—are they not merely contingent states or considerations, wholly dependent on the One who alone is eternal? How does recognizing this shape your sense of self-reliance?
?
When you consider your own attributes (like being a student, a parent, or a believer), do you treat them as stable realities or as fleeting mental considerations? How might this lesson change the way you hold onto them?
?
The passage distinguishes between contingent considerations (like your knowledge) and eternal ones (God's power). What is one area of your life where you are striving after permanence, when only God's power is truly eternal? Will you realign your hope today?

Definition of Mukallaf and Its Conditions
Sanusi defines mukallaf and lists its conditions, with a gloss on jinn and angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who is considered a mukallaf in Islamic theology, the conditions for accountability, and the exceptional cases of jinn, angels, children, and the insane.

Core Idea
Accountability requires adulthood, sanity, senses, and receipt of the divine call.

In Islamic theology, not every human is automatically accountable for religious duties. The concept of 'mukallaf' defines who is legally obliged to follow the Shariah. This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin, with its gloss by al-Ujhuri, clarifies the precise conditions and exceptions. You will encounter the definition, its exclusions, and special rulings for jinn and angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the main definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the core sentence: 'المكلَّفَ : هو البالغ العاقل، سليم الحواس...'. Then read the translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice the key terms: بالغ, عاقل, سليم الحواس.
2
Understand the exclusions
The passage explicitly excludes four categories: the child (even discerning), the insane, the one lacking senses, and the one who never received the call. Identify each and ask why they are excluded.
The discerning child is excluded despite being able to understand.
3
Explore the jinn and angels
The gloss explains that jinn are mukallaf like humans, but from their creation, while angels are not mukallaf on the stronger view. Consider why angels are excluded even though the Prophet was sent to them.
The sending to angels was honorary, not for obligation.
4
Reflect on training vs. obligation
The passage notes that worship requested from a discerning child is for training, not obligation. Think about the wisdom behind this.
This encourages gradual religious practice without burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: عَلَى كُلّ مُكَلَّف أي كلّ فَرْدٍ فردٍ من أفراد المكلفين ولو من الجنّ؛ لأنهم مُكلفون كالإنس، لكن تكليفهم من حين الخلقة، وأما الملائكة؛ فليسوا بمكلفين على الراجح وإن كان النبي الله مُرْسَلاً إليهم؛ لأن إرساله إليهم إنما هو إرسال تشريف لا إرسال تكليف.
واعلم أن المكلَّفَ : هو البالغ العاقل، سليم الحواس - ولو السمع أو البصر فقط ، الذي بَلَغَتْهُ الدعوة. فخرج الصبي ولو مميزاً، والمجنون، وفاقد الحواس، ومَنْ لم تبلغه الدعوة، فليس كل منهم مكلَّفاً وطَلَبُ العبادة من الصبي المميز كالصلاة والصوم ليست لتكليفه بها، بل لترغيبه فيها ليعتادها، فلا يتركها إن شاء الله تعالى.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: مُرْسَلاً إِلَيْهِمْ... إلخ) المراد بإرساله إليهم: أن الله تعالى أمره بتبليغهم ما يجب في حق الله تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز وإن كانوا عالمين بذلك، تشريفاً له بعموم رسالته، وتشريفاً لهم أيضاً بإرسال أفضل الخلق إليهم.
(قوله: سَلِيمُ الْحَوَاسِ) المراد منها: السمع والبصر ، والخارج بذلك مَن خُلِقَ أعمى أصم، أو حصل له ذلك قبل التمييز.
(قوله: وَطَلَبُ الْعِبَادَةِ أي طلب وليّه منه العبادة، لا طلب الله. (قوله: لَيْسَتْ) في بعض النسخ: (ليس)، وهو أولى؛ إذ المحدَّثُ عنه هو الطلب.
(His saying: 'upon every mukallaf' i.e., every single individual among the mukallafūn, even if from the jinn, because they are accountable like humans, but their accountability is from the time of creation. As for the angels, they are not accountable according to the stronger view, even though the Prophet (peace be upon him) was sent to them, because his sending to them is an honorary sending, not a sending of obligation.
Know that the mukallaf is: the adult, sane person, sound of senses – even if only hearing or sight – who has received the call. Thus excluded is the child, even if discerning, the insane, the one lacking senses, and the one whom the call has not reached; none of these is a mukallaf. The request of worship from the discerning child, such as prayer and fasting, is not for his obligation thereby, but rather to encourage him in it so that he becomes accustomed to it, and he does not abandon it if God wills.
Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhūrī:
(His saying: 'sent to them... etc.') The meaning of his (peace be upon him) being sent to them is that God commanded him to convey to them what is obligatory regarding God, what is impossible, and what is permissible, even if they were knowing of that, as an honor for him by the universality of his message, and an honor for them as well by sending the best of creation to them.
(His saying: 'sound of senses') What is meant by them is hearing and sight, and excluded by that is one who was created blind and deaf, or that happened to him before discernment.
(His saying: 'and the request of worship') i.e., the request of his guardian from him for worship, not the request of God. (His saying: 'is not' [laysat]) in some copies: 'is not' [laysa], and it is more appropriate, because the thing spoken about is the request.)
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'mukallaf' (the legally accountable person) in Islamic theology. The matn (original text) states that the judgments apply to every mukallaf. The commentary by al-Ujhūrī first clarifies that the jinn are included in accountability—they are obligated like humans, but their obligation begins from the moment of their creation. Angels, however, are not accountable on the stronger view, even though the Prophet was sent to them; his sending to them was honorary, not for imposing duties. Then the definition is given: a mukallaf is an adult, sane, sound-sensed person (at least hearing or sight) who has received the prophetic call. This excludes children (even discerning ones), the insane, those lacking senses, and those whom the call never reached. The request of worship from a discerning child is not for obligation but to train him. The glosses further explain: 'sent to them' means conveying what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible regarding God; 'sound of senses' specifically means hearing and sight; and 'request of worship' means the guardian asks the child, not God directly. The variant 'laysa' instead of 'laysat' is noted as more correct.

Key Terms
مكلف
تكليف
الجن
الملائكة
دعوة
بالغ
عاقل
سليم الحواس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think angels are also mukallaf (legally accountable) like humans.
Correction: The passage clarifies that angels are not mukallaf on the stronger view; the Prophet was sent to them honorarily, not for obligation.

Checkpoint
Who is excluded from being a mukallaf according to the passage?
A) The adult who has not received the call
B) The discerning child
C) The insane person who has hearing but not sight
D) The jinn

Key Takeaway
A mukallaf is every adult, sane, sound-sensed person who has received the divine call; children, the insane, sense-deprived, and unreached are not obligated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have received the da'wa fully. Are you living today as someone who truly believes you are mukallaf—accountable before Allah for every action? What specific duty have you been neglecting that you will take up right after this reflection?
?
The text says a discerning child is not obligated but is trained. Think of a young person you influence. Are you treating their worship as a burden or as a joyful habit? What one change can you make in how you encourage them so that they grow to love obedience?
?
The jinn were created already accountable, but you were given a childhood of grace. Do you value the years you have as a mukallaf, or do you waste them? Write down one hour this week you will dedicate solely to an act of worship you usually postpone.

The Definition of Knowledge
Sanusi defines knowledge with three conditions and excludes other cognitive states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of knowledge (الْعِلْم) in Islamic theology and what mental states it excludes.

Core Idea
Knowledge is certain belief matching reality from a proof.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that the terms 'ma'rifa' and 'ilm' are synonymous. He then gives a precise definition of knowledge as a three-part concept: certainty, correspondence to reality, and grounding in proof. This definition immediately excludes other cognitive states that might be confused with genuine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage defining knowledge. Notice the three conditions: جزم (decisiveness), مطابقة (correspondence), and دليل (proof).
The definition is from the earlier discussion.
2
Understand what is excluded by the first condition
The first condition (جزم) excludes conjecture (ظن), illusion (وهم), and doubt (شك). Understand each of these terms.
They are three levels of uncertainty.
3
Understand what is excluded by the second condition
The second condition (مطابقة) excludes any decisive belief that does not correspond to reality, like the Christian belief in the Trinity.
The example shows that mere certainty is not enough.
4
Note the inclusion of taqlid
Blind imitation (تقليد) is also excluded because it lacks proof, even if it happens to match reality.
This will be discussed further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أَنْ يَعْرِفَ... إلخ) قد تقدَّم أن التحقيق: أن المعرفة والعِلْمَ مترادفان على معنى واحد، وهو الجزمُ المطابق للواقع عن دليل. فخرج بالجزم: الظن: وهو إدراك الطَّرَفِ الراجح، والوهم: وهو إدراك الطرف المرجوح، والشكُ: إدراك من الطرفين على السَّوَاء. وبالمطابق: غيرُهُ كجَزْمِ النصارى بالتثليث وبما بعده: التقليد، فليس كلّ منها معرفةً ولا عِلْماً، والمُتَّصِفُ بواحدٍ من الأربعة الأول في شيء من العقائد الآتية؛ فهو كافر اتفاقاً. وأما المتَّصِفُ بالأخير - وهو التقليد -؛ فقيل : إنه كافر مطلقاً. وقيل: إنه مؤمن عاص كذلك. وقيل: إنه مؤمن غيرُ عاص كذلك أيضاً. والراجح: أنه مؤمنٌ عاص إن كان قادراً على الدليل، ومؤمن غيرُ عاص إن لم يكن قادراً عليه.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: كَجَزْمِ النَّصَارَى بِالتَّثْلِيث) وفي حاشية الهدهدي: أن النصارى افترقوا فرقتين: فرقة تقول: الله إله وعيسى إله ومريم إله وفرقة تقول: الإله مركب من أقانيم ثلاثة - أي صفات ثلاثة - : أقنوم الوجود أي صفة هي الوجود، وأقنوم العلم، أي: صفة هي العلم وأقنوم الحياة أي صفة هي الحياة، ويسمون أقنوم الوجود بالأب، وأقنوم العلم بالابن وأقنوم الحياة بروح القدس، وهي تسمية اصطلاحية.
His saying: 'to know...' It has preceded that the correct view is that 'ma'rifa' and 'ilm' are synonymous in meaning, which is decisive judgment matching reality from a proof. So by 'decisive judgment' is excluded: conjecture, which is perception of the preponderant side; illusion, which is perception of the weaker side; and doubt, which is perception of both sides equally. And by 'matching reality' is excluded what is other than it, like the Christians' decisive judgment about the Trinity, and what comes after that: blind imitation (taqlid). So none of those is ma'rifa nor ilm. And the one who is characterized by one of the first four in any of the coming beliefs is unanimously a disbeliever. As for the one characterized by the last - which is taqlid - it is said he is a disbeliever absolutely; and it is said he is a believer but a sinner absolutely; and it is said he is a believer not a sinner absolutely as well. The preponderant view is that he is a believer and a sinner if he is able to produce evidence, and a believer not a sinner if he is not able to produce it. Commentary of the distinguished scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: (His saying: 'like the Christians' decisive judgment about the Trinity') In the Hashiya of al-Hadadi: that the Christians divided into two groups: a group says: God is a god, Jesus is a god, and Mary is a god; and a group says: the god is composed of three hypostases - i.e., three attributes: the hypostasis of existence i.e., attribute which is existence, the hypostasis of knowledge, attribute which is knowledge, and the hypostasis of life, attribute which is life, and they call the hypostasis of existence the Father, the hypostasis of knowledge the Son, and the hypostasis of life the Holy Spirit, and these are technical names.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge (ma'rifa/ilm) as certain judgment (juzm) that corresponds to reality (mutabiq) and is derived from proof (dalil). It excludes other mental states: conjecture (zann), illusion (wahm), doubt (shakk), and blind imitation (taqlid). It then discusses the rulings: one who has a decisive false belief (like the Christian Trinity) is a disbeliever by consensus. For taqlid, there are three scholarly opinions, and the preponderant view distinguishes based on whether the person is capable of producing evidence or not. The hashya provides an example of Christian beliefs about the Trinity as an instance of non-corresponding decisive judgment.

Key Terms
المعرفة
العلم
الجزم
الظن
الوهم
الشك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that any strong conviction qualifies as knowledge.
Correction: Knowledge requires not only certainty but also correspondence to reality and a valid proof.

Checkpoint
Which of the following mental states is NOT excluded by the condition of decisiveness (جزم)?
A) Conjecture (ظن)
B) Illusion (وهم)
C) Doubt (شك)
D) Blind imitation (تقليد)

Key Takeaway
Real knowledge must be certain, correspond to reality, and be based on proof; all other mental states fail to meet these criteria.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you consider yourself a 'person of knowledge'? Be honest: do your beliefs about Islam meet all three conditions? Are you certain, does your belief match reality (i.e., is it based on truth as revealed), and is it derived from proof? Where do you fall short, and what will you do today to begin moving from taqlid to certainty?
?
What deeply held conviction do you have that you've never critically examined? Could it be like the Christians' certainty in the Trinity—decisive but false? How can you ensure your certainties are grounded in revelation and proof?
?
This lesson says taqlid is not knowledge. In which areas of your deen—prayer, creed, ethics—are you unknowingly following blind imitation? Will you commit to studying one proof from the Qur'an or Sunnah for a belief you currently hold by imitation?

The Ruling on Taqlid and False Beliefs
Sanusi discusses the consequences of having false certain beliefs and the status of taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on the status of blind imitation (taqlid) in matters of faith, and why false decisive beliefs like the Christian Trinity constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
Blind faith can be sinful or permissible depending on the ability to reason.

After defining knowledge, Sanusi addresses the theological implications. He explains that having a certain but false belief, like the Christian Trinity, makes one a disbeliever by consensus. For taqlid (following without proof), there are three positions, with the preponderant view distinguishing between those capable of proof and those not.

Guided Path
1
Read the rulings on false decisive belief
The passage states that anyone with a decisive belief that does not match reality (like the Trinity) is unanimously a disbeliever.
This applies to the other excluded states as well.
2
Understand the three opinions on taqlid
Read the three different views on whether a muqallid is a disbeliever, a sinful believer, or a non-sinful believer.
These are scholarly positions.
3
Learn the preponderant view
The preferred opinion distinguishes based on whether the person is able to produce evidence or not.
This shows the importance of ability in moral responsibility.
4
Examine the example of Christian Trinity
The hashya explains two Christian views of the Trinity, showing an example of non-matching decisive judgment.
This clarifies the example given in the main text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله: كَجَزْمِ النَّصَارَى بِالتَّثْلِيث) وفي حاشية الهدهدي: أن النصارى افترقوا فرقتين: فرقة تقول: الله إله وعيسى إله ومريم إله وفرقة تقول: الإله مركب من أقانيم ثلاثة - أي صفات ثلاثة - : أقنوم الوجود أي صفة هي الوجود، وأقنوم العلم، أي: صفة هي العلم وأقنوم الحياة أي صفة هي الحياة، ويسمون أقنوم الوجود بالأب، وأقنوم العلم بالابن وأقنوم الحياة بروح القدس، وهي تسمية اصطلاحية.
His saying: 'to know...' It has preceded that the correct view is that 'ma'rifa' and 'ilm' are synonymous in meaning, which is decisive judgment matching reality from a proof. So by 'decisive judgment' is excluded: conjecture, which is perception of the preponderant side; illusion, which is perception of the weaker side; and doubt, which is perception of both sides equally. It then discusses the rulings: one who has a decisive false belief (like the Christian Trinity) is a disbeliever by consensus. For taqlid, there are three scholarly opinions, and the preponderant view distinguishes based on whether the person is capable of producing evidence or not. The hashya provides an example of Christian beliefs about the Trinity as an instance of non-corresponding decisive judgment.

Key Terms
التقليد
التثليث
الكفر
الإيمان
العصيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any form of taqlid is acceptable without distinction.
Correction: The preponderant view says taqlid is sinful only if the person is able to seek proof.

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant view, when is a muqallid (blind follower) considered sinful?
A) Always sinful
B) Never sinful
C) Sinful only if he is capable of seeking evidence
D) Sinful only if he follows a false belief

Key Takeaway
Blind imitation (taqlid) is not necessarily disbelief; its sinfulness depends on the person's capability to access evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or the afterlife simply because you were taught it, without ever questioning or seeking proof? How does the distinction in this passage between those able and unable to produce evidence challenge you to take responsibility for your own faith?
?
The passage describes the Christians' false certainty about the Trinity as kufr. Are there any Islamic beliefs you hold with absolute certainty that you have never verified from the Quran or authentic Sunnah? Would you be comfortable if your certainty was based solely on taqlid?
?
If you are capable of learning the proofs for the oneness of Allah and the prophethood of Muhammad, are you actively doing so, or are you content with blind imitation? What step will you take today to move from taqlid to a faith based on evidence?

The Definition of Knowledge in Islamic Theology
Sanusi defines ma'rifah/ilm as decisive belief conforming to reality based on evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of knowledge (ma'rifah/ilm) in Islamic theology and the three conditions that distinguish it from other mental states.

Core Idea
Knowledge is decisive belief that matches reality and is based on evidence.

Sanusi begins by establishing that ma'rifah and 'ilm are synonymous terms for a specific type of belief. He then lays down three essential conditions that any belief must meet to count as knowledge. This foundational definition is critical because it allows us to distinguish true knowledge from conjecture, doubt, and imitation, which are all common in theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to how Sanusi defines knowledge with three conditions.
The three conditions are decisive belief (juzm), conformity to reality (mutaabiq), and being based on evidence (an dalil).
2
Identify what is excluded
Notice that conjecture (dhann), illusion (wahm), doubt (shakk), and false certainty (like the Christians' belief in the Trinity) are excluded because they fail one of the conditions.
The first three fail the condition of decisive belief (they are not certain), while the Christian example fails conformity to reality.
3
Understand the consensus on the first four
Sanusi states that whoever holds one of the first four states (dhann, wahm, shakk, or false juzm) in any matter of creed is a disbeliever by consensus.
This shows how important correct knowledge is for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أَنْ يَعْرِفَ... إلخ) قد تقدَّم أن التحقيق: أن المعرفة والعِلْمَ مترادفان على معنى واحد، وهو الجزمُ المطابق للواقع عن دليل. فخرج بالجزم: الظن: وهو إدراك الطَّرَفِ الراجح، والوهم: وهو إدراك الطرف المرجوح، والشكُ: وهو إدراك من الطرفين على السَّوَاء. وبالمطابق: غيرُهُ كجَزْمِ النصارى بالتثليث وبما بعده: التقليد، فليس كلّ منها معرفةً ولا عِلْماً، والمُتَّصِفُ بواحدٍ من الأربعة الأول في شيء من العقائد الآتية؛ فهو كافر اتفاقاً. وأما المتَّصِفُ بالأخير - وهو التقليد -؛ فقيل: إنه كافر مطلقاً. وقيل: إنه مؤمن عاص كذلك. وقيل: إنه مؤمن غيرُ عاص كذلك أيضاً. والراجح: أنه مؤمنٌ عاص إن كان قادراً على الدليل، ومؤمن غيرُ عاص إن لم يكن قادراً عليه.

(قوله: كَجَزْمِ النَّصَارَى بِالتَّلِليث) وفي حاشية الهدهدي": أن النصارى افترقوا فرقتين: فرقة تقول: الله إله وعيسى إله ومريم إله وفرقة تقول: الإله مركب من أقانيم ثلاثة - أي صفات ثلاثة - : أقنوم الوجود أي صفة هي الوجود، وأقنوم العلم، أي: صفة هي العلم وأقنوم الحياة أي صفة هي الحياة، ويسمون أقنوم الوجود بالأب، وأقنوم العلم بالابن وأقنوم الحياة بروح القدس، وهي تسمية اصطلاحية.
His saying: 'That one knows... etc.' It has already been stated that the verified position is that ma'rifah and 'ilm are synonymous for one meaning, which is decisive belief (juzm) conforming to reality, based on evidence. Excluded by 'decisive belief' are: conjecture (dhann), which is perception of the stronger side; illusion (wahm), which is perception of the weaker side; and doubt (shakk), which is perception of both sides equally. Excluded by 'conforming' is other than it, like the Christians' decisive belief in the Trinity, and what comes after it: imitation (taqlid). Thus none of these is ma'rifah or 'ilm. Whoever is characterized by one of the first four in any of the coming beliefs is a disbeliever (kaafir) by consensus. As for one characterized by the last—that is, taqlid—it is said: he is a disbeliever absolutely; it is said: he is a believer but sinful (mu'min 'aasin) likewise; and it is said: he is a believer not sinful likewise. The preponderant view is that he is a believer who is sinful if he is capable of [producing] evidence, and a believer not sinful if he is not capable of it.

(His saying: 'Like the Christians' decisive belief in the Trinity') In the Haashiyat al-Hudhudi: the Christians divided into two groups: one group says: God is a god, Jesus is a god, and Mary is a god; and another group says: the deity is composed of three hypostases (aqaanim)—that is, three attributes: the hypostasis of existence, i.e., an attribute that is existence; the hypostasis of knowledge, i.e., an attribute that is knowledge; and the hypostasis of life, i.e., an attribute that is life. They name the hypostasis of existence the Father, the hypostasis of knowledge the Son, and the hypostasis of life the Holy Spirit—these are conventional names.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage provides a precise definition of knowledge (ma'rifah/ilm) in the context of Islamic theology. The author begins by affirming that ma'rifah and 'ilm are synonymous: both mean a decisive belief (juzm) that conforms to reality (mutaabiq li'l-waaqi') and is based on evidence (dalil). From this definition three elements are excluded: (1) conjecture (dhann) which is preference for one side, (2) illusion (wahm) which is preference for the weaker side, and (3) doubt (shakk) which is equal hesitation. Also excluded by the condition of conformity to reality is any belief that does not match reality, such as the Christians' belief in the Trinity—an example of decisive belief that is false. Following this, the author discusses imitation (taqlid), which is believing without evidence. While the first four states (conjecture, illusion, doubt, and false certainty) are universally considered disbelief in matters of creed, taqlid is debated. The author presents three views: absolute disbelief, belief with sin, and belief without sin. The preferred view distinguishes between one who is capable of acquiring evidence (sinful if he relies on taqlid) and one who is not (not sinful). The passage then includes a note detailing Christian trinitarian doctrines, dividing them into two groups: one that worships three distinct gods (God, Jesus, Mary) and another that sees the Trinity as three hypostases (attributes) within one deity.

Key Terms
المعرفة
العلم
الجزم
الظن
الوهم
الشك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any strong belief counts as knowledge, even if it contradicts reality or lacks evidence.
Correction: The text emphasizes that knowledge requires both conformity to reality and being based on evidence; mere certainty is insufficient.

Checkpoint
What are the three conditions for knowledge (ma'rifah/ilm) according to this passage?
A) Decisive belief, strong evidence, and acceptance by scholars
B) Decisive belief, conformity to reality, and being based on evidence
C) Strong conjecture, conformity to tradition, and community agreement
D) Certainty, popularity, and logical proof

Key Takeaway
True knowledge in Islamic theology is decisive belief that matches reality and is proven by evidence, and the absence of any condition strips a belief of the status of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah, do you ever pause to ask: 'Is this based on evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, or am I just assuming it?' What will you do today to check one of your core beliefs?
?
The passage says that even a strong, unwavering belief (like the Christians' Trinity) is not knowledge if it doesn't match reality. What if you are mistaken about something you are absolutely certain of? Are you willing to examine your certainty?
?
Imitation (taqlid) is excluded from knowledge. Reflect: Have you ever accepted a religious teaching simply because others around you believed it, without verifying it yourself? What is one belief you hold that you have not verified with evidence? How can you change that starting now?

The Ruling on Taqlid in Matters of Creed
Sanusi presents three views on taqlid and the preponderant opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly positions on whether taqlid (imitation) is acceptable in matters of creed and the preferred view that distinguishes between those capable and incapable of evidence.

Core Idea
Taqlid is sinful for those who can find evidence but not for those who cannot.

After defining true knowledge, Sanusi turns to the status of taqlid—believing without evidence. He presents three views on the matter, ranging from absolute disbelief to belief without sin. He then gives the preferred opinion, which takes into account a person's ability to acquire evidence. This discussion is crucial for understanding who is considered a believer in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taqlid
Read the part of the passage beginning with 'As for one characterized by the last—that is, taqlid'. Notice the three views presented.
The three views are: (1) absolute disbelief, (2) believer but sinful, (3) believer and not sinful.
2
Understand the preferred view
Sanusi states the preponderant view: a person who can produce evidence is sinful if they rely on taqlid, but one who cannot is not sinful.
This shows that the ruling depends on ability.
3
Consider the Christian example
The follow-up note describes the Christians' trinitarian beliefs as an example of false decisive belief, not taqlid. It separates the two issues.
The Christian example illustrates non-conformity to reality, not taqlid.
