The Multiple Meanings of 'Nafs'
Sanusi enumerates the meanings of 'nafs' and the commentator clarifies the intended sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'nafs' has multiple lexical meanings, and that in theological discussions about Allah's attributes, it refers specifically to His essence (dhāt).

Core Idea
One word, many meanings: 'nafs' means essence, blood, pride, and punishment—but only essence matters here.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a potential confusion: the word 'nafs' (often translated as 'self' or 'soul') has several distinct meanings in Arabic. Before discussing Allah's attributes, it is crucial to know which meaning is intended. This passage lists the main lexical meanings and identifies the relevant one for kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall enumeration.
2
Identify the four meanings
Locate each of the four meanings listed: essence (dhāt), blood (dam), pride (anafa), and punishment ('uqūba). Note the examples given for each.
The commentator explicitly marks 'essence' as the intended meaning here.
3
Understand the theological import
Reflect on why Sanusi starts with this lexical clarification. In kalam, terms must be precisely defined to avoid misunderstanding about Allah's attributes.
This prevents reading 'nafs' as a body part or emotion when applied to Allah.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how a passage about Allah's 'nafs' should be interpreted. Test your understanding with the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن النفسَ تُطْلَقُ على معان كثيرة، منها: الذات، وهو المراد هنا، ومنها: الدَّمُ، وهو المراد في قولهم: ما لا نَفْسَ له سائلةٌ لا يُنَجِّسُ الماءَ، ومنها: الأَنْفَةُ، وهي المرادَةُ في قولهم: فلان لا نَفْسَ له، ومنها: العقوبة، قيل: وهي المرادة في قوله تعالى: (وَيُحَذِّرُكُمُ اللَّهُ نَفْسَهُ) (١) أي: عقوبَتَهُ إلى غير ذلك.
And know that 'nafs' is applied to many meanings: among them: the essence (dhāt), and that is intended here; and among them: blood (dam), and that is intended in their saying: 'that which has no flowing blood does not make water impure'; and among them: pride (anafa), and that is intended in their saying: 'so-and-so has no nafs'; and among them: punishment ('uqūba), it is said: and that is intended in His statement (may He be exalted): 'And Allah warns you of His nafs' (3:30) i.e. His punishment, and so forth.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'nafs' by listing its various lexical meanings. The source text (matn) states that 'nafs' is used for many meanings, then enumerates four: first, 'essence' (dhāt), which is the intended meaning in this theological context; second, 'blood' (dam), as in the juristic rule about impure liquids; third, 'pride' (anafa), as in the phrase 'he has no nafs' meaning he lacks self-respect; fourth, 'punishment' ('uqūba), citing the Qur'anic verse where God’s 'nafs' is interpreted as His punishment. The word 'etc.' indicates there are further meanings beyond those listed.

Key Terms

ذات (dhāt)
دم (dam)
أنفة (anafa)
عقوبة ('uqūba)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'nafs' always means 'soul' or 'self' in a uniform sense.
Correction: The passage shows 'nafs' has multiple distinct meanings; in kalam it specifically refers to essence (dhāt), not soul, blood, pride, or punishment.

Checkpoint
Which meaning of 'nafs' is intended in the context of discussing Allah's attributes?
A) Blood
B) Pride
C) Essence
D) Punishment

Key Takeaway
The term 'nafs' has multiple uses, but in kalam it refers to Allah's essence (dhāt), not soul, blood, pride, or punishment.

Understanding Sifat al-Ma'ani and the Attribute of Power
Defines sifat al-ma'ani and qudra with idafa types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani), the nature of the idafa in that term, and the attribute of power (qudra) as an existent attribute subsisting in Allah's essence.

Core Idea
The attributes of meanings are defined by a specifying idafa, and power is an eternal existent attribute enabling creation and annihilation.

Sanusi introduces the terminology 'sifat al-ma'ani' (attributes of meanings) for seven divine attributes. He clarifies that the grammatical construction (idafa) in this term is specifying, not explicative. He then defines the first of these attributes: power (qudra), describing it as an existent attribute through which all possibilities are effected. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how kalam scholars categorize God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. Notice the technical terms and definitions.
The Arabic text is cut off at the end due to page limits.
2
Identify the two types of idafa
Look for the distinction between specifying (mukhasisa) and explicative (bayaniyya) idafa. The first has absolute generality-specificity; the second has generality-specificity from one perspective.
Example: 'arak tree' vs 'iron ring'.
3
Understand the definition of qudra
Power is defined as an existent attribute by which the creation and annihilation of every possible thing is effected, pointing to its eternal relational suitability.
This connects to the doctrine of God's omnipotence.
4
Reflect on the glosses
Consider al-Ajhuri's criticism of an earlier answer and al-Anbabi's clarification about the idafa being specifying in this discipline but explicative from a linguistic perspective.
These glosses help refine the understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
:(قوله: تُسَمَّى : صِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي بالإضافة التي للبيان، وضابطها: أن يكون بين المضاف والمضاف إليه عموم وخصوص بإطلاق كما في: شَجَرُ أَرَاكِ، لا الإضافة البيانية وضابطها: أن يكون بين المضاف والمضاف إليه عموم وخصوص من وجه، كما في: خاتَمُ حديدٍ، وعُلِمَ من ذلك أن بين الإضافتين مغايرة، وهو الصحيح. وقيل: إنهما بمعنى واحدٍ كما هو موضح في محله. (قوله: وَهِيَ) أي: السبعُ صفات التي تسمى: صفاتِ المعاني. (وقوله: الْقُدْرَةُ) هي صفةً وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى يتأتى بها إيجاد كلّ مُمْكِن وإعدامه إشارة إلى تعلقها الصُّلُوحِيَ القديم. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: وأُجِيبَ... إلخ) هذا الجواب بعيد عن العقول وكذا ما قبله. (قوله: بِمَعْنى وَاحِدٍ وهو الإضافة التي بين طرفيها عموم وخصوص سواء كان بإطلاق أو وجهياً. - تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: بِالْإِضَافَةِ الَّتِي لِلْبَيَانِ) أي: إن نظرَ للمعاني في هذا الفن، وأما إن نظرَ لها من حيث عمومها لمدلولات الألفاظ فالإضافةُ ،بيانيةً، وكل هذا قَبْلَ التسمية، وإلا فصفَاتُ المعاني: عَلَمٌ مُرَكَّبٌ مقصود لفظة هنا بدليل كَوْنِه مفعولاً ثانياً لـ (تسمى). تأمل. (قوله: يَتَأَتَى بِهَا إِيجَادُ كُلِّ مُمْكِن ... إلخ) المراد بالإيجاد ما يشمل الإثبات؛ لتدخل
(His statement: 'They are called attributes of meanings' – with the iḍāfa which is for specification (al-iḍāfa al-mukhaṣṣiṣa). Its rule is that between the mudāf and mudāf ilayh there is absolute generality and specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ bi-iṭlāq) as in 'shajaru arāk' (arak tree), not the explicative iḍāfa (al-iḍāfa al-bayāniyya) whose rule is that between them there is generality and specificity from one perspective (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh) as in 'khātamu ḥadīd' (iron ring). And it is known from this that there is a difference between the two iḍāfas, and that is correct. And it is said that they are synonymous as explained in its place. (His statement: 'And they are') – i.e., the seven attributes that are called attributes of meanings. (And his statement: 'Power') – it is an existent attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya) subsisting in His essence, exalted be He, by which the bringing into existence of every possible and its non-existence is effected, pointing to its eternal relational suitability (taʿalluquhā al-ṣulūḥī al-qadīm). Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'And it was answered... etc.') This answer is far from minds, and likewise what preceded it. (His statement: 'In one meaning' – that is the iḍāfa in which there is generality and specificity between its two ends, whether absolute or from one perspective. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'With the iḍāfa which is for specification') – meaning: if one considers the meanings in this discipline; but if one considers them in terms of their generality for the significations of words, then the iḍāfa is explicative. And all of this is before technical naming; otherwise, 'attributes of meanings' is a compound term whose wording is intended here, as evidenced by it being the second object of 'they are called'. Reflect. (His statement: 'By which the bringing into existence of every possible... etc.') – the meaning of 'bringing into existence' (ījād) includes affirmation (ithbāt) so that it includes...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of key terminology. It begins by explaining the phrase 'attributes of meanings' (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) with two types of iḍāfa (construct phrase). The first iḍāfa is called 'iḍāfa al-mukhaṣṣiṣa' (specifying) where the relationship is absolute generality and specificity, like 'arāk tree' (one species). The second is 'iḍāfa bayāniyya' (explicative) where the relationship is generality and specificity from one perspective, like 'iron ring' (ring made of iron). The correct view is that they differ; but some say they are synonymous. Then it identifies that the seven attributes referred to are these 'attributes of meanings'. Then it defines the attribute of power (qudra) as an existent attribute subsisting in God's essence, by which the creation and annihilation of every possible occurs, referring to its eternal relational suitability (taʿalluq ṣulūḥī). The gloss by al-Ajhuri criticizes an earlier answer (likely the one in lesson 3) as 'far from minds'. The note by al-Anbabi clarifies that the iḍāfa in 'ṣifāt al-maʿānī' is considered specifying when viewed in kalām, but explicative when considered from a linguistic perspective; before technical naming, it is a compound term. Finally, it explains that 'ījād' (bringing into existence) includes affirmation (ithbāt), though the text cuts off.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
القدرة
الإضافة البيانية
الإضافة المخصصة
التعلق الصلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The idafa in 'sifat al-ma'ani' is a simple grammatical possessive, not indicating a specific logical relationship.
Correction: The preferred view is that it is a specifying idafa (mukhasisa) where the relationship is absolute generality and specificity, not merely grammatical possession.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of iḍāfa is used in 'attributes of meanings' (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) according to the preferred view?
A) Explicative (bayāniyya)
B) Possessive (milkiyya)
C) Specifying (mukhaṣṣiṣa) with absolute generality and specificity
D) Metaphorical (majāziyya)

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'sifat al-ma'ani' uses a specifying idafa, and power is an existent eternal attribute enabling creation and annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes God's power as an eternal existent attribute by which He effects every possible thing. How does knowing that God's power is not like your ever-changing ability — always perfectly capable from eternity — change the way you trust Him with something you are currently powerless over? What small step can you take today to act on that trust?
?
The definition of 'sifat al-ma'ani' depends on a specific grammatical relationship. If a mere grammatical detail can clarify such a profound theological concept, what does that say about the precision of the language of revelation? How does that challenge your own carelessness with words about God? Will you commit to thinking before speaking about matters of faith?
?
The text says the bringing into existence includes 'affirmation' (ithbat). God's power not only creates but sustains. Is there an area of your life where you have been acting as if God's power only started things but is not needed now? What would change if you acknowledged His constant sustaining power in that area today?

Motion and Rest: Accidents or Relational?
Al-Bajuri explains the meaning of motion and rest and the problem of considering them accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion and rest are considered i'tibari (relational) matters rather than true accidents in kalam.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are relational, not existential accidents.

Al-Bajuri clarifies a technical point in kalam: although motion and rest are constantly observed in bodies, they are not like color or sound. Kalam reserves 'accident' for existential attributes. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the definitions of motion and rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from explanation to scrutiny.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لكن في جَعْلِهما من الأعراض نَظَرٌ' — this signals the problem.
2
Understand the definitions of motion and rest
The passage gives two sets of definitions. Identify them: one based on transfer, another on first occurrence. Note how rest is defined as the opposite or negation.
3
Grasp the problem with calling them accidents
Al-Bajuri says 'arad is specific to existential matters. Compare motion and rest to blackness/whiteness. Why don't they fit? Because they are relational (i'tibari).
This is the core insight: motion and rest depend on a relation to space.
4
Apply the i'tibari concept
Consider examples: a body moving from A to B. The 'motion' is not a thing you can point to like color; it's the relation of change. That's why it's i'tibari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله)) مِنْ حَرَكَةٍ وَسُكُونٍ وَغَيْرِهِمَا) بيان للأعراض الحادثة، وإنما خَصَّ الحركة والسكونَ بالتصريح بهما؛ لأن ملازمة الأجرام لهما ضرورية لكلّ عاقل، لكن في جَعْلِهما من الأعراض نَظَرٌ؛ لأن الأعراضَ جمع عَرَضٍ، وهو خاص بالأمر الوجودي كالسَّوَاد والبياض، ولا كذلك هما؛ لأن الحركة هي انتقالُ الجِرْمِ من حيّز إلى حيّز آخَرَ، والسكونَ ضده. وقيل الحركة: هي الحصول الأول في غير الحيّز الأول، والسكونُ ما عدا ذلك. وكلّ من الانتقال وضدّه، أو الحصولِ الأول في غير الحيّز الأول وما عداه أمرٌ اعتباري
His saying: 'from motion and rest and other than them' is an explanation of temporal accidents. He specified motion and rest explicitly because the concomitance of bodies with them is necessary for every rational person. However, there is scrutiny in making them accidents, because 'a'rad' (plural of 'arad) is specific to existential matters like blackness and whiteness, but they are not like that; because motion is the transfer of a body from one space to another, and rest is its opposite. It is also said: motion is the first occurrence in other than the first space, and rest is other than that. And both transfer and its opposite, or the first occurrence in other than the first space and what is other than it, are i'tibari (relational) matters.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual clarification by al-Bajuri. He first states that when the author (Sanusi) says 'from motion and rest and other than them', he is explaining the temporal accidents. He specifies motion and rest because it is obvious to every rational person that bodies are always accompanied by either motion or rest. However, al-Bajuri notes a problem: calling motion and rest 'accidents' is questionable because the term 'accident' in kalam is reserved for existential attributes like blackness and whiteness, things that actually exist in the body. Motion and rest, on the other hand, are not existential; motion is the transfer of a body from one place to another, and rest is its opposite. Some define motion as the first occurrence in a new space, and rest as the absence of that. These are relational concepts, not independent existent qualities. Therefore, they are considered i'tibari (relational) rather than true accidents. The passage thus preserves the technical precision of kalam terminology.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
عرض
أمر اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think motion and rest are existential accidents like color or sound.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that accidents in kalam are specifically existential (e.g., blackness). Motion and rest are relational (i'tibari) because they involve a relation between a body and space, not an inherent attribute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, why is there scrutiny in making motion and rest accidents?
A) Because they are not necessary for bodies.
B) Because accidents are specifically existential matters, while motion and rest are i'tibari.
C) Because they are not observable.
D) Because they are not temporal.

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are relational (i'tibari) concepts, not existential accidents, preserving the precision of kalam terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bajuri points out that motion and rest are not existential accidents but relational (i'tibari). How often do you mistake relational changes—like moving from one status to another—for actual, existential transformation in your soul? What would it take for you to focus on real existential change before Allah?
?
The passage highlights that even the most obvious accompaniment of bodies (motion and rest) is not truly an accident in the kalam sense. What 'obvious' attributes of your life (like your busyness or your stillness) might be mere relations, not defining realities? Are you ready to let them go?

The Author's Interpretation of Uluhiyya by Necessary Implication
Bajuri explains the author's derivation of uluhiyya, ilah, and shahada by necessary implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author (Sanusi) defines uluhiyya and ilah through necessary implication (lazim) rather than literal meaning, and how this leads to the meaning of the shahada.

Core Idea
The author defines uluhiyya as God's self-sufficiency and all else's dependence on Him.

Sanusi's interpretation of the key term 'uluhiyya' is not the common one. He defines it by what necessarily follows from it: that the deity is self-sufficient and all else depends on Him. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows how it connects to the shahada.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's derivation carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins by justifying his earlier statement and then derives the meaning of ilah from the meaning of uluhiyya.
The derivation is based on logical necessity (luzum).
2
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: if uluhiyya means self-sufficiency, then ilah means the one who is self-sufficient, and the shahada means there is no self-sufficient except Allah. Understand each step.
This is an interpretation by lazim, not by the literal meaning (mutabiqi).
3
Compare with a literal interpretation
Reflect on how this differs from the common literal interpretation. The author's choice is deliberate, as will be clarified in the next lesson.
The literal interpretation would be 'being worshipped by right'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: إِذْ مَعْنَى الْأُلُوهِيَّةِ... إلخ) تعليل لقوله: (ويجمع معاني هذه العقائد... إلخ)، وقد فَرَّعَ المصنف على ذلك قوله: (فمعنى لا إله إلا الله... إلخ)؛ لأنه يلزم من كون معنى الألوهية استغناء الإله عن كلّ ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما عداه إليه: أن معنى الإله المستغني عن كلّ ما سواه المفتقِرُ إليه كل ما عداه، وإذا كان معنى الإله ما ذُكِرَ ؛ كان معنى لا إله إلا الله لا مستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ. فتلخص أن معنى الألوهية استغناء الإله عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، ومعنى الإله المستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، ومعنى لا إله إلا الله: لا مستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، هذا ما ذكره المصنف هنا. والمشهور: أن معنى الألوهية كؤن الإله معبوداً بحق، ويلزم من ذلك: استغناؤه عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، ومعنى الإله المعبود بحق، ويلزم من ذلك: أنه مُسْتَغْنٍ عن كلّ ما سواه... إلخ، وإذا كان معنى الإله ما ذكر؛ كان معنى لا إله إلا الله: لا معبود بحق إلا الله، ويلزم من ذلك: أنه لا مستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ. إذا علمت ذلك عَلِمْتَ أن ما ذكره المصنف من التفاسير تفسير باللازم لا بالمعنى المُطَابِقِي، وإنما اختار التفسير باللازم؛ لأن اندراج معاني العقائد المذكورة فيه أظهرُ منه في المعنى المطابقي، وبذلك يندفع ما ادعاه بعضُ الفرق الضالة من أن المصنف لم يَعْرِفُ معنى الكلمة المشرفة، وإلا؛ لما فسرها بما ذكر.
(His saying: 'Since the meaning of uluhiyya... etc.') is a justification for his saying: 'And it combines the meanings of these articles of faith... etc.' And the author derived from that his saying: 'So the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah... etc.' because it necessarily follows from the fact that the meaning of uluhiyya is the self-sufficiency of the deity from all other than Him and the dependence of all other than Him upon Him, that the meaning of ilah is the one who is self-sufficient from all other than Him and to whom all other than Him are in need. And if the meaning of ilah is as mentioned, then the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah is: there is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. So it is summarized that the meaning of uluhiyya is the self-sufficiency of the deity from all other than Him... etc., and the meaning of ilah is the one who is self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc., and the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah is: there is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. This is what the author mentioned here. And the well-known (view) is that the meaning of uluhiyya is that the deity is worshipped by right, and it necessarily follows from that His self-sufficiency from all other than Him... etc., and the meaning of ilah is the one worshipped by right, and it necessarily follows from that that He is self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. And if the meaning of ilah is as mentioned, then the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah is: there is no one worshipped by right except Allah, and it necessarily follows from that that there is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. If you know that, you know that what the author mentioned of interpretations is an interpretation by the necessary implication (lazim) and not by the literal meaning (mutabiqi). And he only chose the interpretation by lazim because the inclusion of the meanings of the mentioned articles of faith in it is more apparent than in the literal meaning. And by that, what is claimed by some misguided sects that the author did not know the meaning of the noble word, otherwise he would not have interpreted it as he did, is refuted.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of uluhiyya (divinity) and ilah (deity) as presented by the author (Sanusi). It begins by showing that the author's interpretation is based on necessary implication (lazim). The meaning of uluhiyya is defined as God's self-sufficiency from all else and the dependence of all else on Him. From this, the meaning of ilah is derived as the one who is self-sufficient and to whom all are in need. Then the meaning of the shahada is 'There is no self-sufficient from all other than Him...' The passage then contrasts this with the more famous interpretation: uluhiyya means being worshipped by right, which necessarily implies self-sufficiency. The author's choice of the interpretation by lazim rather than the literal meaning is justified because it more clearly encompasses the five articles of faith mentioned earlier. Finally, this refutes the criticism of some deviant sects who claim the author was ignorant of the true meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah'.

Key Terms
الألوهية
الإله
اللازم
لا إله إلا الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the author's interpretation is the literal meaning of uluhiyya.
Correction: The author's interpretation is by necessary implication (lazim), not literal. The literal meaning is 'being worshipped by right'.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of uluhiyya according to the author's interpretation?
A) The deity is worshipped by right
B) The deity is self-sufficient from all else and all else depends on Him
C) The deity is powerful
D) The deity is merciful

Key Takeaway
The author defines uluhiyya as God's self-sufficiency and all else's dependence, derived by necessary implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is absolutely self-sufficient and you are completely dependent on Him, why do you often feel anxious about things that are not in your control? How can repeating this meaning of the shahada change your daily approach to worry?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah' in your prayers, do you truly acknowledge that only Allah is independent? In what specific areas of your life—such as seeking approval, money, or relationships—do you still behave as if something else is self-sufficient?
?
The lesson shows that 'ilah' means the one who is needed by all. If you truly believed this, how would it affect the way you ask for help from others? Would you turn to Allah first? What is holding you back from doing that consistently starting today?

The Popular Interpretation and Refutation of Critics
Bajuri contrasts the popular interpretation with the author's and refutes critics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the popular interpretation of uluhiyya as 'being worshipped by right' and how the author's choice of the lazim interpretation refutes critics who accuse him of ignorance.

Core Idea
The author deliberately used lazim interpretation to better incorporate the five articles of faith, not because he was ignorant.

Sanusi's interpretation faced criticism from some deviant sects who claimed he misunderstood the shahada. But as Bajuri explains, the author consciously chose the path of necessary implication because it more clearly integrates the five articles of faith than the literal interpretation. This lesson contrasts the two interpretations and shows how the author's choice was both intentional and sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the popular interpretation
Read the second part of the passage starting with 'And the well-known (view) is...'. Understand how the popular view defines uluhiyya as being worshipped by right, and how it necessarily implies self-sufficiency.
The popular interpretation is the literal meaning (mutabiqi).
2
Understand the author's defense
Read Bajuri's explanation that the author's interpretation is by lazim, not mutabiqi, and that he chose lazim because it more clearly includes the meanings of the five articles of faith mentioned earlier.
This shows the author's deliberate choice.
3
See the refutation of critics
Notice how this explanation refutes the claim of misguided sects that the author did not know the meaning of the shahada. The author's interpretation is valid and strategic.
The critics are refuted by showing the author's intentional use of lazim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(His saying: 'Since the meaning of uluhiyya... etc.') is a justification for his saying: 'And it combines the meanings of these articles of faith... etc.' And the author derived from that his saying: 'So the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah... etc.' because it necessarily follows from the fact that the meaning of uluhiyya is the self-sufficiency of the deity from all other than Him and the dependence of all other than Him upon Him, that the meaning of ilah is the one who is self-sufficient from all other than Him and to whom all other than Him are in need. And if the meaning of ilah is as mentioned, then the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah is: there is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. So it is summarized that the meaning of uluhiyya is the self-sufficiency of the deity from all other than Him... etc., and the meaning of ilah is the one who is self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc., and the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah is: there is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. This is what the author mentioned here. And the well-known (view) is that the meaning of uluhiyya is that the deity is worshipped by right, and it necessarily follows from that His self-sufficiency from all other than Him... etc., and the meaning of ilah is the one worshipped by right, and it necessarily follows from that that He is self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. And if the meaning of ilah is as mentioned, then the meaning of La ilaha illa Allah is: there is no one worshipped by right except Allah, and it necessarily follows from that that there is no self-sufficient from all other than Him... etc. If you know that, you know that what the author mentioned of interpretations is an interpretation by the necessary implication (lazim) and not by the literal meaning (mutabiqi). And he only chose the interpretation by lazim because the inclusion of the meanings of the mentioned articles of faith in it is more apparent than in the literal meaning. And by that, what is claimed by some misguided sects that the author did not know the meaning of the noble word, otherwise he would not have interpreted it as he did, is refuted.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of uluhiyya (divinity) and ilah (deity) as presented by the author (Sanusi). It begins by showing that the author's interpretation is based on necessary implication (lazim). The meaning of uluhiyya is defined as God's self-sufficiency from all else and the dependence of all else on Him. From this, the meaning of ilah is derived as the one who is self-sufficient and to whom all are in need. Then the meaning of the shahada is 'There is no self-sufficient from all other than Him...' The passage then contrasts this with the more famous interpretation: uluhiyya means being worshipped by right, which necessarily implies self-sufficiency. The author's choice of the interpretation by lazim rather than the literal meaning is justified because it more clearly encompasses the five articles of faith mentioned earlier. Finally, this refutes the criticism of some deviant sects who claim the author was ignorant of the true meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah'.

Key Terms
الألوهية
الإله
اللازم
المطابقي
لا إله إلا الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some misguided sects claimed the author did not know the meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
Correction: The author knew the literal meaning but deliberately chose the interpretation by lazim because it more clearly incorporates the five articles of faith.

Checkpoint
Why did the author choose to interpret the shahada by necessary implication (lazim) rather than by the literal meaning (mutabiqi)?
A) Because it is easier to understand
B) Because it more clearly includes the meanings of the five articles of faith
C) Because he did not know the literal meaning
D) Because the literal meaning is invalid

Key Takeaway
The author's choice of lazim interpretation was deliberate to encompass the five articles and refute critics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone criticized for their understanding of Islamic concepts, do you rush to judge them without investigating their reasoning? How can this lesson's exposure of the critics' hasty accusation guard you against the same error?
?
The author deliberately chose a less obvious interpretation to serve a deeper purpose. Is there an area of your deen where you cling to a literal understanding while missing the broader spiritual implications? What would it mean to adopt a 'lazim' perspective that connects the text to the core of your faith?
?
Misguided sects used the author's interpretation to claim ignorance. Have you ever been misunderstood or unfairly labeled by others? How does the author's reasoned and calm refutation inspire you to respond when your intentions are questioned?

The Meaning of al-Uluhiyya and Interpretation by Necessary Implication
Al-Bajuri clarifies Sanusi's interpretation of al-uluhiyya as by necessary implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Sanusi interpreted the Shahada using necessary implication rather than its exact literal meaning, and how this refutes a common misconception.

Core Idea
Sanusi's interpretation of la ilaha illa Allah is by necessary implication, not ignorance.

Al-Bajuri clarifies a subtle but important point: when Sanusi defines al-uluhiyya as independence from all else, he is not giving the primary meaning but a necessary implication. This choice was deliberate to better include the full range of beliefs. This lesson walks through Sanusi's interpretation, the more common one, and why the necessary implication is more inclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice how Al-Bajuri presents two interpretations.
Pay attention to the terms 'lazim' and 'mutabiqi'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find where Al-Bajuri states Sanusi’s interpretation and the well-known interpretation. What is the key difference?
One focuses on worship, the other on independence.
3
Understand 'tafsir bi al-lazim'
Al-Bajuri explains that Sanusi’s interpretation is by necessary implication. Reflect on why an interpretation by necessary implication might be chosen.
Think about what beliefs are implied.
4
See the refutation
The passage ends by refuting a deviant sect’s claim. How does the choice of lazim interpretation defend Sanusi?
It shows he knew the meaning but chose a more comprehensive expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتلخص أن معنى الألوهية استغناء الإله عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، ومعنى الإله المستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، ومعنى لا إله إلا الله: لا مستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، هذا ما ذكره المصنف هنا. والمشهور: أن معنى الألوهية كون الإله معبوداً بحق، ويلزم من ذلك: استغناؤه عن كل ما سواه... إلخ، ومعنى الإله المعبود بحق، ويلزم من ذلك: أنه مُسْتَغْنٍ عن كلّ ما سواه... إلخ، وإذا كان معنى الإله ما ذكر؛ كان معنى لا إله إلا الله: لا معبود بحق إلا الله، ويلزم من ذلك: أنه لا مستغني عن كل ما سواه... إلخ. إذا علمت ذلك عَلِمْتَ أن ما ذكره المصنف من التفاسير تفسير باللازم لا بالمعنى المُطَابِقِي، وإنما اختار التفسير باللازم؛ لأن اندراج معاني العقائد المذكورة فيه أظهرُ منه في المعنى المطابقي، وبذلك يندفع ما ادعاه بعضُ الفرق الضالة من أن المصنف لم يَعْرِفْ معنى الكلمة المشرفة، وإلا؛ لما فسرها بما ذكر.
So it is concluded that the meaning of al-uluhiyya is the independence of the god (al-ilah) from everything other than Him... etc., and the meaning of al-ilah is the one independent from everything other than Him... etc., and the meaning of la ilaha illa Allah: no one is independent from everything other than Him... etc. This is what the author mentioned here. And the well-known (view) is that the meaning of al-uluhiyya is that the god is worshipped rightfully, and it follows from that: His independence from everything other than Him... etc., and the meaning of al-ilah is the one worshipped rightfully, and it follows from that: that He is independent from everything other than Him... etc. And if the meaning of al-ilah is what was mentioned, then the meaning of la ilaha illa Allah is: no one is worshipped rightfully except Allah, and it follows from that: that there is no one independent from everything other than Him... etc. When you know that, you know that what the author mentioned of interpretations is interpretation by necessary implication (tafsir bi al-lazim), not by the exact meaning (al-ma'na al-mutabiqi), and he only chose interpretation by necessary implication because the inclusion of the meanings of the mentioned beliefs in it is more apparent than in the exact meaning, and by that is repelled what some of the misguided sects claimed, that the author did not know the meaning of the noble word, otherwise he would not have interpreted it as he did.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of al-uluhiyya and addresses the interpretation of the Shahada. Al-Bajuri first states Sanusi's interpretation: al-uluhiyya means the independence of God from everything other than Him, al-ilah means the one independent, and la ilaha illa Allah means no one is independent except Allah. Then he presents the more well-known interpretation: al-uluhiyya means that God is worshipped rightfully, which necessarily implies His independence. He then shows that Sanusi's interpretation is by way of necessary implication (lazim) rather than the exact primary meaning (mutabiqi). Sanusi chose the lazim interpretation because it more clearly includes the meanings of all the beliefs that follow. This also refutes the accusation by some deviant sects that Sanusi did not know the meaning of 'la ilaha illa Allah'—he did, but he chose the interpretation that best conveys the included beliefs.

Key Terms
الألوهية
الإله
لا إله إلا الله
اللازم
المطابقي
التفسير باللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claimed that Sanusi did not know the meaning of la ilaha illa Allah because he interpreted it in terms of independence rather than worship.
Correction: Al-Bajuri explains that Sanusi interpreted by necessary implication, which is a valid and more inclusive method, thus refuting the accusation.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi choose to interpret 'la ilaha illa Allah' by necessary implication (al-lazim) rather than the exact meaning (al-mutabiqi)?
A) Because the exact meaning is unknown.
B) Because the necessary implication includes the meanings of the beliefs more clearly.
C) Because he was following the Baghdad school of grammar.
D) Because he wanted to refute the deviant sects.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's interpretation of the Shahada is a valid interpretation by necessary implication, chosen for its comprehensiveness, and does not indicate ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Bajuri shows that Sanusi's interpretation is chosen for its comprehensiveness, not ignorance. Is there any area of your own faith where you have dismissed a deeper or more comprehensive understanding as unnecessary? What would it cost you to embrace that deeper meaning today?
?
The passage refutes an accusation against a scholar. Have you ever accused or dismissed someone's understanding of a core Islamic belief without investigating their intent or method? How can you guard your heart against such hasty judgments?

The Two Testimonies: Their Definition and Unity
Al-Bajuri defines the two testimonies and explains the singular pronoun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the two testimonies of faith and why they are treated as a single unit in Islamic creed.

Core Idea
The two testimonies are one in meaning; faith requires both together.

This lesson clarifies what is meant by 'the two words of testimony' in the matn. Al-Bajuri explains that it is the meaning of the testimonies that is included, and then defines them explicitly. He also addresses a grammatical point: why a singular pronoun is used to refer back to both sentences. This subtle point underscores the inseparable unity of the Shahada in Islamic belief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the phrase 'inclusion of the two words of testimony'
The commentator clarifies that it is the meaning, not the literal words, that is included. This is a key distinction for proper creedal understanding.
2
Learn the exact formulation of the two testimonies
The passage states them as 'La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammad Rasul Allah'. Notice they are two distinct sentences.
3
Examine the grammatical shift from dual to singular
The matn uses dual initially but then a singular pronoun. The commentary explains why: because faith is only attained by both together, so they are treated as one unit.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
Understand that the two testimonies are inseparable; affirming one without the other is insufficient for faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: تضمن كلمتي الشهادة أي معناهما؛ لأن المتضمن لذلك إنما هو معناهما لا هما نفسهما كما تقدم، والمراد بتضمن المعنى لذلك: كونه بحيث يؤخذ منه على ما تقدم بيانه وليس المراد به دلالة التضمن كما مر. والمراد بكلمتي الشهادة: لا إله إلا الله محمد رسول الله، وثناهما؛ لأنهما جملتان: الأولى: لا إله إلا الله والثانية: محمد رسول الله، فجعل كل من الجملتين كلمة وأفرد فيما بعد حيث أعاد عليهما الضمير مفرداً؛ لأن الجملتين كالكلمة الواحدة باعتبار كون الإيمان لا يحصل إلا بمجموعهما، ولا يكتفى فيه بإحداهما عن الأخرى.
His statement: 'the inclusion of the two words of testimony' means their meaning, because what includes that is their meaning, not the two words themselves as mentioned before. And what is meant by the inclusion of the meaning for that is that it is such that it is taken from it as previously explained, and what is meant by it is not the implication of inclusion as passed. And what is meant by the two words of testimony is: 'There is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. And he dualized them because they are two sentences: the first is 'There is no god but Allah' and the second is 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. So he made each of the two sentences a word, and then afterwards he singularized when he referred back to them with a singular pronoun because the two sentences are like a single word considering that faith is only attained by both of them together, and one of them is not sufficient without the other.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and doctrinal clarification of the Shahada (the two testimonies of faith). The commentator first explains that when the matn says 'the inclusion of the two words of testimony', it means the inclusion of their meaning, not the literal words themselves — this is a point about how the creed is contained in the Shahada. Then he defines the two testimonies explicitly as 'La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammad Rasul Allah' and notes that they are referred to in the dual because they are two distinct sentences. However, the commentator observes that the matn later uses a singular pronoun when referring back to them. The reason given is that the two sentences are treated as one single word because faith (iman) is not valid unless both are affirmed together; one cannot suffice without the other. This underscores the integral unity of the two testimonies in Islamic creed.

Key Terms
شهادة
تضمن
كلمة
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two testimonies are separate and one can be accepted without the other.
Correction: The passage states that faith is not attained except by both together, and one is not sufficient without the other.

Checkpoint
Why does the matn use a singular pronoun when referring back to the two testimonies?
A) Because they are grammatically feminine
B) Because they are considered as one word since faith requires both
C) Because the singular pronoun is a stylistic choice
D) Because the two sentences are identical

Key Takeaway
The two testimonies of faith are inseparable; affirming both together is essential for valid iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the two testimonies as one inseparable unit in your daily life, or do you sometimes prioritize one over the other? What would change if you truly lived the Shahada as a single declaration?
?
When you recite the Shahada, do you feel the weight of both its parts? How can you ensure that your faith in Allah and your obedience to the Prophet ﷺ are equally present in your actions today?
?
If your iman could be 'one word' uniting tawheed and sunnah, what is one specific area where you need to bring them together? Commit to fixing that today.
